INDIA
PM  POWER  ENEATION PROJECT
Loan and Proiect Summary
iorrowers           National Thermal Power Corporation Limited
(NtPC)
Guarantors          India, acting by its President
Amounts             US$400 million
Terms:              20 years, Including five years grace, at the Bank's
standard variable interest rate. NTPC would bear the
foreign exchange and Interest rate risks.
Guarantee Fees      The Government of India (GOI) would charge a guarantee
fee of 12 on the outstanding amount of the Bank Loan.
Project
Description:        The project comprisess (a) Generation CaLacity Additions:
a five year time-slice of NTPC's least cost investment
program of new coal and gas-based power stations for
which full funding has not yet been arranged; (b) Private
Sector Components support to NTPC to un4ertake several
joint venture operations; and (c) EnviLronmental
Strengthening and Resettlement and Rehabilitations
implementation of an Environmental Action Plan (RAP)
which Includes environmental upgrading projects, training
and technical assistance for the strengthening of NTPC's
environmental and R&R management capability and the
Implementation of the RAP.
benefits8           The proposed project would: (a) improve commercial
discipline In the power system in India through the
implementation of NTPC'8 new commercial and investment
policies; (b) help NTPC meet its targets for capacity
additions through Increased mobilization of funds fro
internal resources, domestic and foreign capital markets
as well as from the private sector through joint venture
operationsa (c) upgrade environmental performance of WTMC
power stations and make its new power plants
environmentally more sustainable; and (d) strengthen
NSPC's environmental and resettlement and rehabilitation
management capability. Of India's proposed inwestments
in thermal generation capacity, those of NMPC are among
the lowest-cost options and warrant support when overall
investment resources are constrained and an increasing
share of the total funds available is devoted to
rehabilitatlon of ezisting generating facilities and
strengthening the transmLssion and distribution system.



- v '
It would help meet the energy needs of the Indian economy
at a relatively low cost and high efficiency to support
the far reaching macro-economic and structural adjustment
program.
Risks*              The project does not pose any particular technical risks
as proven technologies will be utilized and project
implementation will follow practices well-established at
NTPC. Its Investment program is constrained more by the
availabilityof financing than by the demand for
electricity or by its implementation capability. The
core investment program has been prepared taking Into
account NTPC's capability to raise resources through
internal cash generation and from the capital markets.
NTPC's main risk is financial and relates to its ability
to recover its charges from its clients, the State
Electricity Boards. As long as it recovers, the level
and structure of the tariff will safeguard its financial
position and provide adequate self-financing for the
implementation of its investment program. Special
attention has therefore been paid during project
processing to developing satisfactory commercial policies
and arrangements which would ensure satisfactory revenue
recovery, and would allow NTPC to divert supply fron non
paying customers and direct investments to the
financially viable SEBs. The environmental risks
associated with this project will be minimized through
the implementation of an Environmental Action Plan (ZAP),
which Includes an institutional strengthening component
and provides for implementation of improved resettlement
and rehabilitation policies. Implementation of the ZAP
will ensure that NTPC power stations will operate in an
environmentally sustainable manner.

MIA
NTPC POUER GENERATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
T. SECTORAL CONTEXT
Eneray - Economy Interfaces
3.1       Develo2Lent Dolicy, energy production and use.  India's long-
standing development policies sought to promote self sufficiency and growth
through a combination of public ownership and control of key strategic
institutions, planned investment, administered pricing of key coumodties,
control over domestic markets, and protection from external competition. This
strategy resulted in a capital- and energy-intensive pattern of development
and inefficient production and use of energy - in a capital and, in relatlve
terms, energy-poor country. Cost-plus pricing provided no incentive for
efficiency to fuel producers and bulk power generators; below-cost retail
prices of electricity have caused substantial losses and severe financial
difficulties to many power utilities. Agricultural and residential sectors
have enjoyed artificially low energy prices, and have had no reason to focus
on efficiency. Cost-plus pricing in protected markets and lack of competitive
pressures have provided little incentive for effieient use of energy to
industries and commerce, even if energy supplies to them have generally been
more appropriately priced.
1.2       New Directions in Energy Strategy.  The energy sector has accounted
for nearly 30Z of Plan expenditures. This heavy investment has yielded
substantial results in terms of increases in output, but earlier achievements
in meeting energy demand increasingly from domestic sources have not been
sustained. Indigenous energy development has not kept up with the increasing
demand and is now being curtailed by public finance constraints. In the face
of the current and medium-term budgetary difficulties, the sector can no
longer rely on net budgetary support by the Government of India (GOI) along
the lines of the past. GOI's new energy strategy reflects the financial
realities and has begun to pay more attention to efficiency issues is
production, use and pricing of energy. ihereas previous Plans gave highest
priority to accelerated exploitation of domestic energy resources, the Zighth
Plan (FY93-FY97) places more emphasis on sustainable development, giving
greater attention to development of renewable energy, demand management,
conservation and efficiency. GOI is also relying to a greater extent on
private initiative and investment. However, given the long gestation period
of most energy projects and the time required for the consumers to respond to
GOI's ongoing economic liberalization efforts, further deterioration of the
supply/demand situation and increasing reliance on energy imports, with
adverse balance of payments implications, are expected in the coming years.
Unless India is able to implement fundamental changes in the structure,
management and financing of the sector and shift to a less energy-intensive
development path, increasing cost of energy and supply limitations threaten
future economic growth.
1.3       Power Situation and Challenge.  Over the past decade, electricity
consumption in India increased at an average annual rate of about 91, but with
a per capita level of about 270 Kwh per annum, remains among the lowest in the
world. Power shortages persist and, in FY92, were equivalent to about 9t of



- 2 -
total energy and 18Z of peak capacity,yequirements. As pointed out in a
recent Bank study of the power sector- in India, increased emphasis needs
to be placed on improving the efficiency of supply, consumption and pricing of
electricity.  Supply capacity expansion alone will not substantially improve
the critical power demand/supply situation and is likely to be financially
unsustainable. A major challenge for 001 in the power sector In the 1990.
will, thus, be to strike a better balance between supply expansion and
efficiency improvement. Demand is expected to continue to expand at an
average growth rate of 8? per annum and massive investments in rehabilitation
and new power supply facilities are required even taking into account
efficiency improvements. GOI funding of the sector's investment program is
likely to be insufficient and the sector will have to rely increasingly on
internal resources of state aud central government-owned utilities as well as
private investment.
rgranization and Regulation of the Power Sector
1.4       Regulatory Framework.  Responsibility for electricity supply is
shared constitutionally between GOI and the states. The Electricity (Supply)
Act (The Act) of 1948 as amended, provides the overall regulatory framework
for the sector. The Act charges the Central Electricity Authority (CIA) with
developing a national power policy and planning, coordinating and regulating
sector development. The Act also empowers the central government, currently
the Ministry of Power (MOP), to make rules for carrying out CeA's objectives,
by notification in the Official Gazette. The Act created state electricity
boards (SEBs) and entrusted them with primary responsibility for public power
supply as well as for related state-level regulation - i.e., the SEBs were
envisioned as largely self-governing entitiev, including tariff setting (para.
1.6). The Industrial Policy Resolution of 1956 subsequently defined aspects
of generation and distribution which were to be the exclusive responsibility
of the states and the once significant role of private utilities has gradually
diminished (para. 1.8). While it is recognized that various sections of the
Act could be modernized, following recent amendmnts (para 1.8), there are no
fundamental legal obstacles for the power sector entities to operate on a
commercial basis or for the private sector to enter into the sector.
1.5       Central Sector.   The central government has been actively involved
in power development since the 19708, by establishing its own utilities to
complement the efforts of the states in order to accelerate power development
in India. GOI currently ownss (a) several generating companies including the
National Thermal Power Corporation (NTPC), the National Hydroelectric Power
Corporation (NHPC); and (b) the Power Grid Corporation of India (POWERGRID), a
transmission company and grid operator. These utilities were incorporated
under the Companies Act - the Electricity Act covers them as generating
companies. Tariff setting principles for the centrally-owned entities are
approved by MOP, actual tariff proposals are reviewed by CIA as the regulatory
authority and notified for implementation by MOP. Tariffs are determined on a
cost plus basis and provided the utilities collect tariff revenues, their
.1/ Long Term Issues in the Indian Power Sector CLTIPS), December 1991. The
study was prepared in 1990-91, with financing fromz the Overseas Development
Administration (ODA) of the United Ringdon and the United States Agency for
International Development (USAID). It was formally discussed with 0OI in
February 1992.



W3..
financial viability Is ensured to the extent that they would be able to fully
cover their operation cost including debt service. More ambitious financial
objectives have been adopted for POWIERORID (under Ln. 3577-IN for the
POWSRGRID System Development Project) and for NTPC under the proposed project.
GOI also owns two power sector financial intermediaries, Power Finance
Corporation (PFC) and the Rural Electrification Corporation (REC). The
Ministry of Von-Conventional Energy Sources administers 60I's renewable energy
program, which seeks to supplement conventional po.-er supply with alternative
energy systeme, e.g., mini-hydro, biomass, wind and solar energy, as vell as
meet the decentralized energy needs of the rural sector.
1.6       State Sector.  The states own the SEBs and the State Generating
Companies (SGC), which together generate about 75X of electricity supply and
provide most of the distribution to final consumers. The ElectricIty Act
explicitly requires the SEBs to operate 'in the most efficient and economical
manner' and also mandates the SEBs to adjust their own tariffs so as to
achieve a minimum (and no maximum) return after interest of 32 on average
fixed assets in operation. The Act furthers (a) allows a state to specify a
higher minimum return for its SEB; (b) specifies that interest on loans from
the state governments is to be paid by the SEB only after meeting the minimum
return; and (c) introduces the concept of subventions to be paid by the states
to help the SEns meet the minimum return requirement, in compensation for
using the SE  as8 administratively convenient vehicles for agricultural input
subsidies. For most SEBs meeting the 32 after-interest return would allow
them to cover operating cost and debt service, provided rural electrification
subsidies are fully paid in time and bill collection is reasonable. Most SEBs
do not, however, reach the Act's statutory minimum returns and their aggregate
operating losses ranged from Rs. 3.2 billion in FY86 to Rs. 14.7 billion in
FY92 (in total about US$3 billion). Net internal cash generation has been
negative every year since FY86 and all capital expenditures were financed from
borrowing and/or state budgetary resources. "his is attributable to the poor
operational efficiency of the SE8s, low tarix-a amd even more importantly,
to political interference by the state governments. Although the Act grants
the state utilities considerable autonomy, in practice they must obtain state
government approval (often at the highest political level) for most major
decisions including those on investments, tariffs, borrowing, salary and
personnel policies.
1.7       SEBs and SOCs are grouped into five regional interconnected power
systems in the Eastern, Northern, North-Eastern, Southern and Western regions
of India. GOI created the Regional Electricity Boards (REWs) in 1964 to bring
together the concerned SEBs and central and joint sector utilities to
coordinate system operations in their respective regional grids and also
entrusted CEA with the development and operation of Regional Load Dispatch
Centers (RLDCs) to support the REBs. In 1991, GOI strengthened the authority
of the REBs and RLDCs by an amendment of the Electricity Act requiring
generating companies and licensees to follow their instructions. The transfer
of RLDCs and CEA's operational responsibilities to PONERGRID, to be completed
by December 31, 1995, was agreed under Ln. 3577-IN approved on March 23, 1993.
1.8       Private Utilities.  At independence, private utilities and
licensed local authorities together provided about 80Z of public
electricity supply. Most licensees have since been taken over by the SE8s
when their licenses expired, and no new licenses have been granted since 1956.
OAly five private utilities remains   BSES Ltd. (BSIS) and Tata Electric



-4-
Companies (TEC) in Bombay, Ahmedabad Electricity Company (AEC), Surat Electric
Company, and Calcutta Electric Supply Corporation Ltd (CzSC) in Calcutta.
Their generation capacity totals about 2,800 MW and additional plants are
under construction. India's long-standing private power policy was radically
reversed in 1991* power was removed from the list of activities reserved for
the public sector in the Industrial Policy Resolution and the Act was amended
to lift many of the regulatory disincentives to private investment in the
power sector. This is fully in line and consistent with GOI's economic
liberalization objertives, to tap the financial, technical and managerial
capabilities of the private sector into areas previously reserved to the
public sector. India now allows full ownership of power companies by the
private sector (local and foreign), an extended period of license of 30 years
with 20-year renewals and increased financial returns. Provision is also made
in the Act for private generating companies and captive plants to sell powmr
to the SEBs. Additional incentives were introduced in GOI budget for FY94,
includings (a) a reduction of import duty on power projects to 20?; and (b) a
five-year tax holiday for new private (as well as public sector) power
projects. These changes have resulted in remarkable business development
activity by private developers (para. 1.19).
Electricity SuDSl.L Demand and Tariffs
1.9       India's power system has an installed capacity of over 70,000 MW,
in size comparable with that of the United Kingdom. In FY92, this system
generated about 283,500 Gwh, about 70X from coal-fired power stations, 25?
from hydro stations, and SZ from gas, oil and nuclear stations. Supply has
expanded quicklys in FY82 installed capacity was only 32,350 MW, and
generation 114,000 Gwh. In parallel with the growth of supply, operational
efficiency hbs improved. For example, the plant load factor has increased
from 442 to about 552 over the last ten ),ars and the rate of coal consumption
by power stations has been cut by about 10X. These improvements reflect a
strengthening of plant maintenance and operations and are commendable in view
of the deteriorating quality of coal supply. The actual improvements have,
however, been uneven among the SEBs and reflect the performance and increasing
share of NTPC, which in FY92 provided about 212 of India's total and almost
30X of its thermal power supplies. The operational efficiency of NTPC's
plants has consistently surpassed that of the state utilities. Power station
auxiliary consumption in India exceeds 10?, technical and commercial losses in
transmission and distribution are estimated at 222 of net generation and
interruptions and reductions in supply and voltage are common. The poor
quality and unreliability of public supplies cause consumers to purchase
costly back-up generating capacity: an estimated 6,250 MV of captive
generating facilities are operated by industries.
1.10      Industry consumes almost half (472) of all electric power;
agriculture uses 272; residences 16Z; commerce S2; and public services the
balance. The growth in electricity demand has been accelerated by relatively
low power tariffs which have fostered wasteful end-use of energy. Average
retail tariff rates have increased only slightly since FY82 in real terms and
remain at about 50 of the long-run marginal cost (LRMC). Increases in real
costs (particularly for fuel and wages) have offset the increases in
efficiency and real tariffs. In the meantime, tariff differentials among
consumer groups have widened. Rates to Industrial consumers are in most
states now close to, and in some cases even above, LRMC. Agricultural
tariffs, on the other hand, have fallen in absolute terms to an average of



-5 -
about paise 16/Kwh in FY92 and cover less than 10 of LRMC. Partly as a
result of this subsidization, agriculture's share In total consumption has
grown from about 17Z in FY82 to about 27Z in FY92. Compounding the problem is
the poor collection from farmers, and the high transmission and distribution
losses for rural supply. They contribute to an increasing financial burden
imposed by agriculture on the power sector.  A similar situation applies to
the residential uses of electricity.
Past Bank Group Operations in the Power Sector
1.11      Lending.  The Bank group has made 36 loans (US$6.8 billion) and 19
credits (US$2.4 billion) for power projects in India (Annex 1.1)1!. IFC has
made five investments totalling Us$203 million to AEC and TEC in FY89, TIC and
CESC in PY90, and BSES in FY91. The physical implementation of most Bank
power projects in India has proceeded slowly, but eventually, after
significant initial delays, has broadly met expectations. While the
disbursement profile of the portfolio is well in line with the Bank average
1ower disbursement profile. loan and credit disbursements of the Indian
portfolio continue to show large outstanding balances (US$3.8 billion as of
March 31, 1992). These are due primarily to the long construction periods for
generation projects compounded by frequent delays in procurement, in foreign
exchange and import license clearances by the various ministries and in
counterpart fund releases from state governments. Undisbursed balances have
been pushed up further by frequent cost underruns on major equipment
contracts. The latter are due, in some instances, to the softening of
international markets in the mid-19809 and, in others, to the depreciation of
the Rupee. The Bank has reviewed its power loan portfolio for India and has
since December 1991 canceled US$864.2 million.
1.12      Past Stratesy.  Over the last decade, IBRD followed a three-pronged
strategy in its lending to the power sector in India. Firstly, it supported
agencies owned by GOI, STPC in particular, as a means of effecting sector-wide
Improvements. Secondly, it financed a selected number of SEBs whose
management and state government appeared to be committed to reforms. Finally,
and in close cooperation with IFC, it financed existing private power
utilities to improve their financial and economic efficiency and to encourage
00I to lower entry barriers for new investors. The success of this strategy
has been uneven, in line with fundamental sectoral weaknessess despite some
progress, SEBs in general did not respond to GOI's initiatives and there was
no mechanism to enforce compliance or elicit cooperation, even in case of non-
payment for central sector electricity.
1.13      Experience from Previous NTPC OCerations. As of January 31, 1993,
the Bank Group had invested US$ 3 billionJ in 15 operations with NTPC. The
total undisbursed balance under five ongoing projects amounted to US$461
2/ Loan and credit amounts are net of cancellations.
S/ Net of cancellations and after transfer of the transmission assets to
POVEIGRID (para 2.6).



- 6 -
million. Project completion reports for the first nine NTPC operationsV
and the Performance Audit Reports for the first seven recognize the
achievements and success of NTPC in implementation; despite some delays in the
pre-construction stages, NTPC generally completed the projects on schedule and
within budget, reflecting the strong project management capability it has
developed.  Quality control procedures introduced by NTPC helped improve the
quality standards of power equipment supplied by Indian manufacturers. On the
negative side, the financial health of NTPC has been threatened by the
accumulation of receivables from the SEBs. Intensified collection afforts and
central appropriations (direct payments by 600 from budgetary support intended
to the concerned states) have reduced NTPC's accounts receivable and further
improvements have been achieved after the approval of NTPC's new investment
and commercial policies (para. 2.2) by the Government in Oct ber 1992 after a
prolonged dialogue with the Bank.  The Bank's latest Audit i/ and Project
Completion Reports noted that its own efficiency notwithstanding, NTPC did not
bring about the expected sectoral improvements. In addition to the above-
mentioned commercial issues, the reports also identified areas for further
improvement for NTPC itself, highlighting the need to pay more attention to:
(a) internal resource mobilization by NTPC for its future investment; (b)
adequacy of coal supply arrangements; (c) environmental impact mltigation and
rehabilitation and resettlement of project affected persons; and (d) pra-
implementation activities including preparation of tender documents and timely
award of contracts. These issues have been discussed with NTPC and have been
incorporated in the design of the proposed project.
1.14      Experience with SEBs.  Beginning in the mid-1980s, IBRD attempted
to improve the performance of SEBs by direct involvement at the state level.
This approach, involving close lending relationship with the SEBs, has been
successful in a few cases, such as Maharashtra. Projects with institutionally
and financially weaker SEBs have not met expectations, forcing the Bank to
apply increasingly strong remedies, up to suspension of disbursement and
subsequent cancellations of loans to the Delhi Electric Supply Undertaking
(DESU) and the Uttar Pradesh State Electricity Board, and threat of
suspensions of loans to the SEBs in the States of Nimachal Pradesh and Kerala.
The Bank's stance has yielded positive responses from some of the concerned
state governments, including substantial tariff adjustments in Nimachal
Pradesh, Kerala and Uttar Pradesh. Tariffs in Uttar Pradesh were, however,
rolled back in June 1992. This event, combined with the failure to implement
agreements to settle current power bills of NMPC and fundamental weaknesses in
the operations of UPSEB forced the Bank to cancel the US$350 million Ln. 2957-
IN for the Uttar Pradesh Power Project in August 1992. The two power loans to
Karnataka were suspended on April 8, 1993 due to non-compliance with financial
covenants and unresolved environmental and resettlement and rehabilitation
issues. Close and frequent supervision of the remaining SEB loans is
necessary to help ensure continued compliance with loan agreements, setting
effectively a limit to the number of the Bank's direct SEE operations.

1.15      Exoerience with Private Utilities.  The Bank and IFC's experience
with the private utilities has been generally satisfactory. Unlike the SIBs,
private utilities have been allowed by their respective state governments to
operate autonomously and in a technically and financially viable manner. They
have not suffered from the Institutional and financial problems of the GOI-
owned entities either, because the power they generate is largely fed into
their own distribution networks which supply financially lucrative urban
residential and industrial consumers.
Power Sector Reform
1.16      GOI Strategy in the Power Sector.  The growth targets of GOI's
economic stabilization and liberalization program will be threatened as long
as power supply constrains industrial development and the financial losses of
the power sector burden public finance. GOI has adopted a three-pronged
approach to remedy the root cause of the sector's efficiency and resource
mobilization problems, the poor performance of the SEDs. First, ¢O! imposes
financial discipline on the SEBs through central agencies (PFC, NTPC and
POVMEGRID); second, GOI presses the states to adjust their power tariffs; and
third, GOI opened the sector to private investment.
1.17      Central Sector Agencies as Agents of Reform.  India's federal
structure makes it difficult to condition central assistance to the states as
budgetary allocations are set by an automatic allocation formula. Within this
overall budgetary and central assistance framework, GO! uses its own agencies
to promote operational and financial improvements in the SEBss
(a) PPC, supported by the Bank, ADB and USAID, will provide resources
only to utilities, which have committed themselves to improve their
performance, under time-bound Operational and Financial Action Plan
(OFAP), endorsed by their state governments and aimed at improving
the utilities' resource mobilization, operational efficiency and
commercialization;
(b) GOl has adopted new investment and commercial policies and
electricity tariffs allowing its main generating utility, NTPC, to
shut-off or restrict power supply if its clients are in default
with their bulk supply agreements (para 2.2). They are designed to
introduce better commercial discipline at SEBs, along with
improving NTPC's own operational and financial performance,
Including improving revenue collection; and
(c) GOI established POWERGRID to: (a) improve the efficiency in power
transmission and system operations, through an extensive
restructuring of the transmLssion sector; and (b) complement lts
policy initiatives to encourage private generation and competition
in power generation.
1.18      Progress at State-Level Tariff Reform.    GOI's intensified
pressure on the states to raise agricultural tariffs has yielded encouraging
results. The State Power Ministers agreed in their April 1992 and
January 1993 meetings to implement a minimum Rs. 0.50tKwh agricultural
tariff (this would bring agricultural tariffs to about 202 of LRMC). At the
moment, seven SEBs (Assam, Haryana, Madhya Pradesh, Maharashtra, Orissa,
Rajasthan and West Bengal) have introduced the new minim= rate. The tariff



reform process has at best only just started and the challenges ahead are
formidable: regular tariff adjustments and better collection practices,
starting with but going well beyond the implementation of the agreed minimum
tariff, are needed to restore the financial viability of SEBes.
1.19      Progress with Private Power.  Faced with a growing power gap and
diminishing public resources available for the sector, GOl has opened in 1991
the power sector to private sector participation in generation, transmission
and distribution. Increased reliance on the private sector will also
contribute to improving financial discipline among the SEBs since private
investment would only go to states with an attractive policy environment and a
financially sound SEB. Following the amendments of the Act (para. 1.8), a
High Powered Board chaired by the Cabinet Secretary was established in October
1991 to promote private investment and accelerate the clearance of projects.
The policy changes and high-level promotional efforts have been successful in
encouraging in power projects. About 43 proposals are currently under active
consideration; for 38 of them, various SEBs have signed Memoranda of
Understanding (HOU) with private developers; eight projects are at an advanced
stage of negotiation. In spite of substantial interest, exploratory activity
and MOUs, no agreements have yet been finalized. Progress in the
implementation of the proposed projects has been slow due to: (a) unresolved
policy and regulatory issues (such as collection and exchange rate safeguard
mechanisms and import tax and duty concessions); (b) unclear administrative
processes (project clearances, CEA's involvement) and insufficient preparatory
work to make these project proposals bankable projects; and (c) the poor
financial position of most of the SEBs.
1.20      Further Actions.  GO1's strategy, though ambitious and demanding,
still constitutes only the first steps to effectively address the sector's
pressing institutional, efficiency and resource mobilization issues. Further
actions are required on two fronts to improve the sector's investment mix and
to accelerate reforms at the state level:
(a) the balance in the sector's investment program between supply
expansion and efficiency improvement needs to be improved. 001's
power sector investment priorities recognize the need to
rehabilitate existing plants and increase investments in
transmission and distribution relative to investments in generation
and the Eighth Plan is an improvement over previous plans In terms
of investment balance. This shift from supply expansion to
efficiency needs to be continued and pursued more vigorously.
Central sector agencies, NTPC in particular, have demonstrated the
capacity to implement power generation projects and operate the
facilities efficiently, well over the record of most SEBs.
Therefore logically the main resource shift would take place at the
state level, from new SED generation projects to transmission and
distribution investments, along with rehabilitation of existing
generation facilities. This would accelerate the realization of
the emerging industry structure outlined in paras. 1.21-23; and
(b) at the state level, the SEB would need to be given the financial
and operational autonomy foreseen in the Act. Up-front actions
should inclu'des (a) financial restructuring to establish a
reasonable debt to equity structure and satisfactory financial
objectives for the SEBes, including an increase in internal cash



- 9 -
generation to make a reasonable contribution to future investment,
(b) tariff adjustments to meet the revised financial objectives
including significant increases in agricultural tariffs; (c)
improvements in billing, collection and payments to central
utilities; (d) adjustments in investment programs from supply
expansion to efficiency; and (e) contracting with the private
sector or NTPC the rehabilitation and operation of poorly
performing stations and other services.
1.21      Emerging Industry Structure.  With the establishment of POWERGRID
(assisted under Ln. 3577-IN), India has taken an important strategic step
towards a functioning national power grid with an increasingly competitive and
coordinated generation and transmission system. The emerging industry
structure would consist oft (a) competing central, state and private
generating companies; (b) POWERGURD as the national grid company; and (c)
state and privately-owned distribution. SEBs are likely for the time being to
remain integrated state-level utilities, but are expected to focus more on
distribution and, with POWERID's assistance on trading and improved
coordination of their system operations. Power purchases from central sector
utilities (NTPC in particular) and competitive procurement of private
generation would account for a steadily increasing share in their overall
supply. (Long-term prospects also include power imports from Nepal). As a
result, the commercial pressures on the SEBs would increase as generators
(both private and public) focus their investments and operations to serve
financially viable and comercially disciplined clients and divert supplies
from non-performers; at the same time generating companies would have to
increasingly compete to sell their output to the performing SEBs.
1.22      In the medium-tarm the role and structure of the state sector and
pace of reform will not remain uniform across India. A few states are
expected to undertake reforms ir. line with the agenda outlined in para. 1.20
and let their SEBs operate along commercial lines. The most progressive
states may also be able to push through more ambitious restructuring,
Involving corporatization of the SIB and splitting them into generation,
transmission and distribution companies, possibly with private sector
participation. It is, however, likely that many states will be unable to
create the broad political consensus, and will choose the status quo and the
resulting continuing deterioration of power supply.
1.23      This industry structure, in particular any significant involvement
of the private sector, will require a workable regulatory framework, which is
only developing. At the central level, CRA is progressively giving up its
operational responsibilities. The Regional Load Dispatch Centers (RLDCs) are
being transferred to POVERGRID) and de-emphasizing its role in supervising
project implementation, to focus on regulatory functions. With increased
competition in generation and eventually in distribution, a key component will
be the regulation of transmission access and pricing.  Ln. 3577-IN includes
technical assistance to examine this issue and review the role of CEA and the
functions of other bodies directly and indirectly involved in transmission and
power trading. As concerns state level regulations, there are no easy answers
and experience from other countries does not provide a one and only dLrectiont
solutions adopted elsewhere range froms heavy regulatory processes with
independent regulatory authorities (e.g. United States) to reliance on
competition with either light incentive regulation (e.g. United Kingdom) or
mainly a threat of government regulatory intervention (e.g. Finland, New



- 10 -
Zealand) with operational and financial autonomy of the utilities. Without
prejudging the evolution of state level regulation in India. a suitable
medium-term target would be to effectively give the SEBs the operational and
financial autonomy envisioned in the Act (para. 1.20).
Rationale and Strategy for Bank Support for Power Sector Reforms
1.24      Coimitment to Reform. As explained above, 001's actions through
NTPC, POWERGRID and PFC and opening the sector to private investment
demonstrate a clear conmitment to reform. GOI's program supports policies for
effective institutional, regulatory and financial reform in line with the
recently approved guidelines for Bank lending to the power Sector. The
following paragraphs measure GOl's reforms against those guidelines and
present ongoing and planned Bank support for the reforms.
1.25      Transparent regulation.  India has in place, in its Electricity
Supply Act, a workable regulatory framework for the sector (paras. 1.4-1.6)
and transmission regulation is being studied under Ln. 3577-IN. Progress has
been made in designing a regulatory framework making private investment in the
power sector more attractive, by amending the Act (para. 1.8). The major
remaining regulatory area which needs to be tackled is that applicable to the
SnIs as in practice the state governments do not allow the SEBs to operate
with the autonomy provided by the Act. The next step in the dialogue with 0OI
on institutional and regulatory arrangements, in particular at the state
level, is an institutional seminar in October 1993 sponsored by the Indian
Energy Operations Division and IENIESMAP.
1.26      Pricing and Demand Side Management. New two part bulk tariffs for
NTPC coal fired stations became effective in November 1992 (para. 2.2).
Further reforms in bulk power and transmission tariffs will be studied and
implemented under Ln. 3577-IN.  At the state level progress on tariff reforms
has been generally unsatisfactory, although GOI has succeeded in making states
begin to adjust agricultural power tariffs (pars. 1.18). Apart from the need
to improve cost recovery and meeting financial targets, major changes are
required in the structure of the SEB tariffs to ensure that it provides
appropriate incentives to conserve energy. Demand side management (DSM) is
being introduced in India and incentives for conservation are only emerging
with the ongoing trade liberalization, which will introduce the required
competitive pressures (paras. 1.1-1.2) and with state-by-state electricity
tariff reform.
1.27      Importation of Services.  As part of its llberalization program,
India has opened up its power sector to entry by foreign investors and import
duties on advanced technology and equipment have been reduced. The use of the
Bank's standard bidding documents, initiated with Maharashtra in 1989 and
since continued with PlC and POVERORID, as well as under the proposed project
with NTPC, will encourage competition and exposure to foreign manufacturers,
contractors and consultants. Compared to most other developing countries,
India has an abundance of educated and trained manpower and the use of foreign
consultants would mainly be required in high-technology projects (such as
POVERGRID's regional power system coordination and control investments) and in
the management of projects of less-efficient SEEs/states.
1.28      Commercialization and Coruoratization.  The centrally-owned
entities in the power sector are all incorporated under the Companies Act and



- 11 -
already enjoy reasonable autonomy in decision making and day-to-day
operations. The main challenge is the application of the commercial policies
now granted by MOP to the central agencies. The process of establishing
commercial contracts between the SEBs and the central utilities is cumbersome,
but progress is being made with strong Bank support under Ln. 3577-IN and the
proposed project. NTPC's new bulk power purchase agreements with the SEBs are
equivalent to the power purchase agreements negotiated with private
generators. Efforts are also being made under these operations to improve
NTPC's and POWERGRID's access to domestic and international capital markets.
As discussed above the dialogue on commercialization and corporatization of
the SEBs has been initiated and the Bank in its future operations will seek to
support those states who wish to commercialize their SEBs (para. 1.30).
1.29      Private investment. India, unlike most developing countries, has a
few large private utilities already in operation (para. 1.8). The Bank has
been actively involved, in close cooperation with IFC. in supporting their
investment programs. Following the opening of the power sector for new
private developers, the Bank is supporting the implementation of the new
policy under the Second Maharashtra Power Project (Ln. 3498-IN, approved in
June 1992) by helping Maharashtra develop its private power policy and
regulatory framework and negotiate contracts with private developers for major
power generation projects. These activities are partly financed by two grants
from the Japan Grant Facility. The Bank is currently preparing a Technxical
Assistance Project for Private Power Development to provide 001 and the
concerned entities the required specialized expert services to help resolve
the remaining policy, regulatory and administrative issues and assist in
project preparation. Under the proposed project the Bank would assist NTPC in
establishing joint ventures with private domestic and foreign investors.
1.30      Bank strategy.  The Country Assistance Strategy seeks and supports
reforms by GOI to address the fundamental operational, financial and
institutional issues in the power sector. The reform process in the power
sector has only just begun and much remains to be done particularly at the
state level. The Bank's leading program will be used to continue the dialogue
and provide some of the incentives needed to make the required tough
decisions. In addition, the Bank will continue to use the management of the
existing portfolio to support the needed reforms. Bank lending is focused on:
(a) promoting domestic and foreign private investments; (b) supporting GOI's
agents of reform, PFC, NTPC and POWERGRID; and (c) assisting SEBs which
perform in a satisfactory manner or have started implementing credible plans
underwritten by their states to improve their performance and finances.
Preparatory work is underway on an SEB Restructuring Project to assist states
willing to undertake up-front reforms towards the corporatization of their
SEBs and a more transparent regulatory processes along the lines of the agenda
described in para. 1.20.
II.  THE BORROWER - NATIONAL THERMAL POWER CORPORATION
Introduction
2.1       Since its creation in 1975,  NTPC has become a major actor in
India's power sector. NTPC commissioned some 30Z of the sector's new
generating capacity over the period PY82-FY92. It is estimated that by the
end of FY93, NTPC operated about 18Z of India's total installed generating



- 12 -
capacity and 262 of the thermal capacity. Its share in India's total power
supplies has reached about 222 of total generation and 29Z of all thermal
generation. NTPC has demonstrated that Government owned power utilities can
be operated at efficiency levels comparable to those of privately owned
utilities In India and well run utilities outside of India. It has managed to
create and maintain a "corporate culture" based on professional pride of doing
things well, through highly motivated staff. The Bank Group's support has
gone much beyond financial assistance and the close relationship betwees NTPC
and the Bank has helped NTPC to become an example to follow in the Indian
power sector through emphasis on (i) long term strategic corporate planning;
(ii) a systems approach as a primary management tool making it possible, at a*
early stage, to combine a high degree of centralization with adequate on-site
authority; (1ii) establishment of a systematic quality assurance program;
(iv) build-up of high quality in-house engineering capability helping to
Identify technical innovations; and (v) implementation of a comprehensive
manpower development program, that contributed to attract the best
professionals, thus keeping the quality of staff at a high level.
Recent Power Sector Policy Reforms Affecting NTPC
2.2       As part of the ongoing dialogue between GOI and the Bank during the
preparation of this project, GOI has introduced a number of policy reforms
aimed increasing commercialization and efficiency of the power system as well
as fostering participation of the private sector in power generation which
will change the way NTPC operates and directs its future investments. These
reforms include:
(a) following the creation of POVERGRID in 1989 (para. 1.2), NTPC's
transmission systems and corresponding liabilities were transferred
to POWERGRTD with effect from April 1, 1992, including associated
personnel (para. 2.6);
(b) new comercial and investment policiess Following directives issued
by MOP in October 1992: (1) NTPC has been permitted to shut-off or
restrict power supply from its concerned power stations, whenever
physically and technically feasible, in case of non-compliance with
the agreed terms, including appropriate payment coverage by letters
of credit tLCs), of the bulk power supply agreements (BPSA) with
the SBss; (ii) if the above is not possible, the defaulting
state(s) would be charged penal rates for drawals (power Imports)
exceeding LC coverage; (iII) for new power stations, NTPC and
POWERGRID have been instructed to design the system in such a way
that it would allow the shut-off or diversion of supplies in case
of non-compliance with the BPSA; (iv) NTPC would delay new
Investments in a state if that state is not in compliance with the
BPSA; and (v) NTPC is permitted to undertake projects in one region
with a substantial part of the output to be allocated to other
regions;
(c) in January 1993, NTPC was authorized to enter Into ioint ventures
with foreian and local private partners and to develop with those
partners power projects either from its own investment program or
those offered by others; and



- 13 -
(d)  a new two part bulk tariff came into effect on November 1, 1992 for
coal fired stations in the Southern, Western and Northern regions
and for the Eastern region with effect from January 1, 1993. New
BPSAs covering both coal fired and gas fired stations are being
signed on a regional basis. (pars. 4.9).
2.3       The Impact of these policy reforms on NTPC is substantial.  It io
now only a generating company and will soon have a transparent contractual
relationship with all its customers.  It is allowed to follow commercial
practices by only supplying customers who pay and by diverting supplies to
paying customers. NTPC cac now manage its portfolio of projects more
aggressively by entering into joint ventures with private partners and selling
electricity across regions. The new two part tariff provides NTPC with
incentives for further increasing its operational efficiency.
Organization and ManaRement
2.4       ITPC, the borrower of the proposed loan, was incorporated as a
Government owned company in 1975 under the Company's Act of 1956. The
Corporation is governed by the general provisions of the Electricity (Supply)
Act, 1948, as amended, and is under the supervision of the Ministry of Power.
It is responsible for designing, constructing, and operating large thermal
power stations and selling the power it generates to the SEBs. NTPC enjoys a
considerable degree of autonoty in its day to day operations (in particular
compared to the SE8s) but like other public enterprises, decisions on tariffs,
anual capital budgets, salary scales, investment program and major financing
decisions are subject to Government approval. Since FY88, NTPC has signed
every year Memoranda of Understanding with GOI requiring it to meet specific
operational and investment objectives during the year.
2.5       NTPC's operational policies are determined by its Board of
Directors which consists of a Chairman and Managing Director, five full time
Directors for Finance, Personnel, Technical, Operations and Projects and six
part time members representing relevant ministries and Government agencies.
The overall organizational structure is based on a corporate headquarters and
five operational regions. In 1991, the Indian Institute of Management,
Ahmedabad, completed a detailed diagnostic study of NTPC's operational
mnAgement policies and culture building process along with a review of
organization structure. NTPC also carried out an internal assessment of the
need for decentralization of its day-to-day operations. As a result, the five
regions - Northern, Western, Southern, Eastern and National Capital Region -
are now each headed by an Executive Director responsible for the management of
power stations. Regional headquarters have been strengthened with a
Commercial and Operational Services group. Project Engineering and
Procurement personnel has been regrouped on the bas1s of various projects
under implementation in the different regions to Improve co-ordination in pre-
award and post award activities. NTPC has a Consultancy Division and has been
increasingly successful in obtaining domestic and international contracts.
Its current organization chart is shown in Appendix 2.1. Two major
organizational changes took place recently namely the transfer of transmlssion
as ets to POWERGRID and the acquisition of the Feroz Gandhi Unchahar Thermal
Power Project (Unchahar project).



- 14 -
2.6       Transfer of Transmission Assets and Liabilities to POWERORID.  NTPC
underwent a major organizational change on August 16, 1991 following GOI's
decision to entrust the management of transmission iLas and associated
engineering and contracts work to POWERGRID prior to the de jure transfer of
the assets. The full manpower of the regional transmission line groups
responsible for construction, operation and maintenance of transmission lines
and about 60Z of the personnel of Power System group at Corporate Center
associated with transmission lines engineering and procurement activities was
transferred to POWERGRID, a total of about 2,229 persons of which 657
executives. On January 8, 1993, the NTPC, KHPC and NEEPCO (Acquisition and
Transfer of Power Transmission Systems) Ordinance, 1993, was promulgated by
the President of India. This Ordinance provides for the transmission systems,
including all the rights, titles and other interests related to the
transmission systems of each of the corporations to be transferred to
POWERORID retroactively, with effect from April 1, 1992. For NTPC a total of
15 Bank loans and credits were involved. All were granted to GOI and onlent
to NTPC. Total net Bank commitments to NTPC are reduced from US$3,694.2
million equivalent to US$3,002.8 million (Annex 2.2). Bank has agreed in
principle for this transfer of assets and loan documentation is being amended.
2.7       On February 14, 1992, NTPC acquired the Unchahar Thermal Power
Project, 2x210 MW belonging to the Uttar Pradesh Rajya Vidyut Utpadan Nigam
(UPRVUN), a State Government Undertaking. The transfer of assets was in lieu
of the receivables outstanding from the Uttar Pradesh SEB. Most arrears
outstanding at that time (Rs. 4,837 million) were eliminated as a result of
the take over and as part of the agreement, nearly all non-executive cadre
(1,164) has been transferred to NTPC. Most of the executives stationed at
Unchahar power station were on deputation from UPSEB and are being gradually
replaced by NTPC executives from other power stations to ensure a smooth
transition. This transaction demonstrate the efforts NTPC has made to reduce
*the level of receivables from one of its principal defaulting clients. The
Unchahbr power station is now being run in the same way as other NTPC power
stations. The second phase of the power station will be constructed by NTPC
utilizing the US$ 160 million assistance from the Asian Development Bank (ADB)
earlier granted to the Government of Uttar Pradesh.
Staffing and Training
2.8       As of March 31, 1992, NTPC employed 21,917 persons, of which 2,066
were employed in corporate head-quarters and 19,851 in the regions. About
6,401 are executives and the remainder are supervisory, technical, support
staff and trainees. Manpower growth has slowed down over the last few years
and total number of staff decreased by about 600 hundred because of the
transfer of transmission assets to POWERGRID, despite the take over of the
Unchahar power station. Quality of NTPC staff is good and continuous training
is provided to upgrade technical and management skills. Where necessary
external recruitment is undertaken to acquire specialized staff skills. The
slow down in NTPC's investment program has caused a certain under-employment
of engineering staff, but they are being deployed in other parts of the
organization.



_ 1S -
2.9       Training.   Since 1981, NTPC provides specialized training in
management and other aspects of power sector operations in its Power
Management Institute (PHI) located in Delhi. In 1991-92, PMI conducted 50
management development programmed Involving 905 participants. Most of the
programs are for NTPC staff, but the PMI also opens its courses to a limited
number of participants from SEBs and other public enterprises. The Bank is
providing financial assistance to the PMI iunder Ln 2845-IN. NTPC owns a
Central Simulator Training Center at Korba, equipped with two full simulators
for 200 and 500 MV coal fired thermal units. A combined cycle gas plant
training simulator will be installed at Xawas. Smaller technical training
centers exist at all project sites.
Generation Facilities and Cperational Performance
2.10      NTPC commenced generating electricity in 1982. By the end of FY92,
NTPC was operating 11,333 MK of capacity ( 9,900 MW coal based and 1,433 MV
gas based, Annex 2.3), generating 61,350 Gwh in the same year, accounting for
21.4Z of India's total generation and 29.42 cf the thermal plant generation.
NTPC also operates the 720 MV GOI owned Badarpur station near Delhi and has
been responsible for the construction and operation of the 270 NV captive
power station established at Korba for the Bharat Aluminum Company, on a
management fee basis. In FY92, NTPC's average plant load factor was 70.172,
14.872 point higher than the all India PLF of 55.32. In the same year, the
availability of NTPC's coal fired stations was 83.52, auxiliary power
consumption 8.08S, specific oil consumption only 1.73 al/Kwh and the heat
rate, a measure of thermal efficiency, was 2,490 kcal/lwh. For NTPC's gas
based stations the average availability was 77X; specific gas consumption 0.25
SMC/Kwh and the heat rate 1,869 kcal/Kwh. (Annex 2.3). Plant efficiency and
reliability parameters compare well with other utilities in the world.
2.11      The good performance of NTPC's stations can be credited to the
sound operation and maintenance practices established within NTPC and
consistent deployment of trained personnel. Operational plant performance is
systematically reported to and analyzed at NTPC's headquarters. Pre-
established maintenance schedules prescribed by manufacturers are strictly
observed and remedial actions are taken when revealed by operating
characteristics. Where necessary assistance is obtained from the
manufacturers.
Environment, Rehabilitation and Resettlement
2.12      NTPC has made considerable efforts in recent years to respond to
increasing environmental requirements, address environmental problems and
improve its Resettlement and Rehabilitation (R&R) programs. During the
preparation of the proposed project, a detailed assessment was made of NTPC's
performance. It was observed that environmental data including data for
monitoring R&R programs were not collected in a systematic way and that
remedial actions were often taken only in response to stricter GOI/State
regulations rather than on the basis of scientifically collected environmental
and socio-economic parameters. Most of NTPC's existing power plants were
designed and built at a time when India's environmental standards were less
stringent than they are today. Therefore NTPC's power stations do not operate
at todays GOI environmental standards, but a program to upgrade the
environmental performance of its existing stations is already in place and
will be implemented as part of the project (para. 2.19). Annex 2.4 gives an



- 16 -
overview of NTPC's current environmental performance and describes the
pertinent GOI and World Bank environmental standards. In August 1992, NTPC
constituted a Committee to review its environmental management needs,
including those for R&R. Their environmental practices were also reviewed by
consultants from British Electric International (SET),UK In November/December
1992. A number of organizational changes were recently  introduced (pars. .14
to 2.19), and once fully implemented, would help to correct the problems
identified.
2.13      Resettlement and Rehabilitation.  RTPC operates in several states
and to ensure a common approach, it formulated in 1991 its own comprebensive
(R&R) policy for project affected families, which was approved by the Ministry
of Power. Although it shared the same objective, it was not fully compatible
with the Bank's guidelines on involuntary resettlement. During project
preparation agreement was reached on a revised NTPC R&R policy, which is
broadly consistent with the Bank's operational directives and applies to all
NTPC operations. The basic objective of the policy is to ensure that after a
reasonable transition period, the affected persons improve or at least regain
their previous standard of living. A number of options like 'land for land'
(if required on a willing/buyer willing/seller basis through a land purchase
committee), training to upgrade skills, allotment of shops with seed money as
loan, self-employment with seed money as grant, jobs with NTPC in the
unskilled and semi-skilled categories etc. are included in the policy. The
policy also provides for the rehabilitation of Project Affected Families
(PAFs) of existing NTPC projects, if required, who have not been able to
regain or improve their previous standard of living. NTPC's latest R&R policy
is reproduced in Annex 2.4.
2.14      NTPC's Current Organization for Ehvironmental Management and R&R.
Eavironmental responsibilities at NTPC corporate headquarters are divided
between three divisions: (i) the Environmental Engineering Group (ERG) in the
Engineering Department under Director (Technical) is responsible for the
environmental aspects and obtaining clearances from regulatory agencies;
(ii) Director (Operations) is responsible for the recently created (September
1991) Environmental Management Group (ERH); this group monitors the collection
and analysis of environment related data of power stations; (iii) the
Personnel and Administration (P&A) division created in February 1993 a
corporate Community Development Group under Director (Personnel) to oversee
all aspects of R&R and to provide support for similar groups at the plant
level. The Corporate Planning (CP) department interacts with all funding
agencies on environmental issues.
2.15      The ERG currently employs about 12 people of various disciplines
and frequently uses outside consultants and expertise of other NTPC
departments. Its main responsibilities are: (i) association in site selection
for new projects with engineering services; (ii) preparation Environmental
Impact Assessments (ETA) for new projects and obtaining clearances;
(iii) finalization of specification of equipments for pollution monitoring;
(iv) special studies relating to environmental problems; (v) interaction with
the Ministry of Environment and Forestry (MOEF) and the State Pollution
Control Boards (SPCBs); and (vi) coordinating the overall EIA studies; and
(vii) provide assistance to the sites in overcoming specific technical
problems related to environment.



- 17 -
2.16      Compliance with environmental regulations and environmental
management used to be the responsibility of the general manager of each power
plant, but environmental data were not provided to headquarters an a regular
basis. The Eavironmental Management Group (DMG) was created In September 1991
and is now staffed with 6 professionals. It is responsible for#
(i) coordination with power stations, regions, engineering divisions on all
environmental matters: (ii) maintenance cf an environmental data bass, trend
analysis of pollution monitoring data and preparation of exception reportss
(iii) environmental auditing of power stations: (iv) providing operational
feedback to Engineering for carryitg out necessary modifications in
existinglfuture power stations; and (v) providing corporate support to
stations through organization of meetings on environmental management, status
of implementation of remedial actions, obtaining management approvals etc.
2.17      The Corporate Community Development Group is responsible fort
(i) supervising the socio-economic studies carried out by consultants:
(i1) finalization of R&R and community development plans for new projects: and
(iii) policy review and follow up and implementation of RR and comumnity
development plans. Socio-economic studies are generally carried out by
external consultants. At the corporate level, the group has presently three
professionals but staff will increase when required, depending on the volume
of work.
2.18      Organization at the Plant level.  The environmental organization at
the plant level mirrors the corporate set-up and in April 1992 Environmental
Management Groups were formed at each operating coal based plant headed by a
full-time Senior Manager, Environmental Management Group, who is
administratively responsible to the Plant General Manager through the head of
Management Services, and coordinates with the ENG at corporate headquarters.
The basic responsibilities of the EIG at the plants includes (i) coordination
with the State Pollution Control Boards (SPCBs9) (ii) pollution monitoring:
and (iii) data analysis and implementation of corrective measures. The PEA
department at various NTPC sites is directly responsible for R&a and community
development plans and interacts with the displaced persons, local NGOs and
authorities. It facilitates land acquisition and is responsible for
compensatory payments. The staffing level varies for each station, but for
example at the 2,000 MW Singrauli Thermal Power station, 42 full time and 65
part time staff vould be involved in environmental protection and RUR
activities.
2.19      To make the operation of NTPC's existing plants and the design of
its new power plants environmentally more sustainable, a comprehensive
Environmental Action Plan (EAP) has been prepared by NMP, in consultation
with the Bank. The RAP covers all NTPC operations and addresses the required
further institutional strengthening of NTPC to deal effectively with
environmental and R&R problems. It includes investments and technical
assistance required to implement remedial actions to bring existing plants in
compliance with the latest GOI and SPCB environmental standards. It also
inciudes socio-economic studies to ascertain the present economic status of
the PAPs of existing NTPC power stations and implement appropriate romedial
action plans. The RAP is discussed in para 3.30 and Annex 3.2.



- 18 -
NTPC Tariffs and the Emerging Bulk Power Market
2.20      NTPC's Original One-Part Tariff.  Since the beginulng of its
generation operations until November 1992, NTPC has charged for its
electricity supply using a one-part tariff structure, which combined fixed
costs (mainly capital costs) and variable costs (mainly fuel costs) into one
energy (Kwh) charge. A separate tariff was determined for each power station,
on the basis of its actual capital and fuel costs, using prescribed normative
operational parameters, as well as a 122 return on equity. Whlile the level of
such tariffs was high enough to enable NTPC to pass on its full costs for each
station and achieve a reasonable overall financial performance, NTPC's one-
part pricing contributed to the operational Inefficiencies in the Indian power
system and some of the tariff parameters were increasingly contested by many
SEBs. The main issues with NTPC's one-part tariff were:
(a) it distorted plant dispatch. Merit-order dispatch would place the
generally more efficient and lower variable cost NTPC plants ahead
of the generally less efficient and higher variable cost SEB
plants. One-part tariff, by adding NTPC plants fixed cost to the
variable cost, made aach additional unit of NTPC power more
expensive to the SEBs than the variable cost of their own plants.
This has resulted in the reluctance of SEB1 to back down their
units in off-peak periods, in spite of their higher variable cost,
and system frequencies well above 50 Hz are not uncomon;
(b) it contributed to the poor grid discipline. There was no mechanism
for penalizing SEEs which took more than their shares of
electricity from central sector plants. This in turn has resulted
In some SEBs getting less than their share, and system frequencies
falling well below 50 Ha during peak periods;
(c) it enabled over-recovery by NTPC. Whenever the plant load factor
(PLF) exceeded the normative parameter used in the tariff
calculation, NTPC effectively over-charged SEEB for its fixed
costs. In addition, as there was no mechanism to compensate NTPC
for backing down its plants, NWPM stations contributed to the high
system frequencies in off-peak periods by continuing the generate
more than required; and
(d) it did not promote efficiency improvements by NTPC. The normative
operational parameters (such as plant heat rates, auxiliary fuel
and electricity consumption, PLF) were not updated and although
regarded ambitious when first set (as the parameters exceeded the
average SE8 performance by a wide margin) over the years JTPC's
performance exceeded these original expectations by a wide margin.
2.21      The New Two-Part Tariff.  A review of NTPC's tariffs was undertaken
in late 1980s by a committee headed by Mr. K.P. Rao, former Member (Economic &
Commercial) of CEA. The K.P. Rao Comnittee proposed the separation of NTPC's
fixed and variable costs in a conventional two-part tariff and making rates
dependent on NTPC's operational performance. These were, In the Indian
context, ground-breaking recommendations. Two-part tariffs have been
introduced since November 1992 for VTPC's coal-fired stations in the Northern,
Southern and Western regions and from January 1993 in the Eastern region. New
bulk tariffs for NTPC's gas-fired stations will be Included with the new
a



- 19 -
BPSAs. With the new tariff parameters, including the recently approved
Increase to 162 In return on equity on NTPC's new Investments, NTPC's tariff
for incremental supply to the SEns (sales from new power stations) will
initially be well in line with its long-run marginal cost. The fixed cost
part of the tariff will, however, slowly (in line with inflation) fall below
LRMC as assets are not revalued. The average tariff revenue from NTPC's
existing stations, resulting from the new two-part tariffs, is below LRMC for
the same reason. The variable cost part of the tariffs of both new and
existing stations is domitated by the GOX-administered prices of coal and gas.
After significant adjustments In recent years, coal prices reflect economic
costs reasonably well; while the recent depreciation of the Rupee has brought
the delivered price of gas down to about 75Z of the targeted fuel oil parity.
Pressure to raise both coal and gas prices is mounting in order to raise
sufficient resources for coal and gas development and help attract private
investment. The two-part tariff policy allows NTPC to pass future fuel price
adjustments to the consumers.
2.22      The new two-part tariffs allows NTPC to recover its costs and earn
a satisfactory return on its investments, subject to meeting performance
targets on plant availability and efficiency and auxiliary fuel and power
consumption:
(a) NTPC has an effective tariff Incentive to generate efficiently and
is penalized for inefficiency. Performance over the efficiency
(heat rate) target enables UTPC to increase its profits as it
retains the resulting fuel cost savings. Performance below the
target reduces its profits as it is not allowed to recover the
additional fuel costs;
(b) NTPC has an incentive to improve the availability of its plants.
Fixed costs are recovered and target return on equity is achieved
only once generation meets the specified availability, calculated
on the basis of a plant load factor adjusted for deemed generation
(defined below). Lower availability initially reduces NTPC's
profits (lower return on equity), and then results in less than
full recovery of fixed costs;
(c) NTPC is compensated for backing down. Deemed generation (amount of
electricity not generated when the plant was available for
generation, but backed down at the Instruction of the Regional Load
Dispatch Center) is taken into account in the calculation of the
PLW for tariff purposes. NTPC no longer has an incentive to
contribute to over-frequency by generating over the system
requirements in off-peak periods; but
td) NTPC has an incentive to exceed the normative availability/PLF
target to help meet system requirements during peak periods (yet
can not over-recover its fixed costs, as the fixed-cost part of the
two-part tariff allows recovery only up to the availability/PLF
tarset.) Performance exceeding this target is rewarded by allowing
an additional profit element in the variable-cost portion of the
two-part tariff. The higher the actual generation during times of
system demand (off-peak periods are covered under the deemed
generation provision), the higher the bcaus per unit of additional
generation.



- 20 -
2.23      The hmerinu   Bulk Power Market and Future Tariff Reforms. The
introduction of the new two-part tariffs by NTPC is an essential element of
India's reform program for power transmission and system operations.   The
two-part tariff structure enables merit-order generation dispatch Irrespective
of contracts between generators and their clients and are a prerequisite In
the development of a competitive market structure in the power sector. Power
trading In India's emerging industry structure (para. 1.21) is expected to be
dominated by capacity contract trading (long-term contracts between generators
such as NTPC and their clients such as $Bs). The generators are able to
recover their fized costs and earn a return an their equity Investments as.
long as their stations are available for generation, and will have no reason
not to back down their stations in favor of a lower variable cost plant to
help mlnimise the overall cost of system operations. At a later stage
capacity contract trading will be supplemented by spot trading in a short-term
market with prices reflecting supply and demand on a short-term basis. NtPC
Is expected to participate in spot trading to the extent that its 8PSAs with
the SBs do not cover the full output of its stations or vhen it diverts
supplies from non-paying clients under its new commercial policy. A study to
help develop bulk power tariff recommendations for capacity and spot trading
has been commissioned under Ln. 3577-IN, for completion by the end of 1993.
The recommendations of the study will be reviewed with GOI and the Bank not
later than June 30, 1994 and thereafter implemented in accordance with tlme-
bound action plans satisfactory to the Bank. The detailed terms of reference
of the study are given in Amnex 2.5.

2.25      Four ongoing projects, three coal-fired (Farakka, Talcher and Dadri
- under the National Capital project) and one gas based combined cycle (GBCC)
(Kawas) plants are funded under ongoing Bank loans. The Dadri and Gandhar
GBCC projects are funded by Rf of Germay and OZCF of Japan, respectively.
The Kshalgaon coal-fired project is executed with financial assistance from
the former USSR. All the above projects would be completed during the 8th
Plan period (PY93-FY97) and NTPC's total installed capacity In operation would
reach 16,625 MV (13,450 MW coal and 3,175 MV gas). NTPC's investment program
also includes remaining capital expenditure on existing stations such as ash
disposal systems, upgrading of electrostatic precipitators and other remedial
environmental projects as well as retention payments.
2.26      NTPC's current investment program includes nine coal-fired power
plants with an aggregate installed capacity of 8,260 NV and one 400 MW gas-
based combined cycle power plant expected to be initiated during the Bighth
Plan (Table 2.2). Salient parameters of these projects are given in Annex 2.6
In addition, NTPC is planning to develop several projects as a minority
partner in joint ventures with private companies (para 3.9). During the Ninth
Plan, NTPC expects to start construction of seven projects (six, coal-fired,
and one gas-based combined cycle) for a total capacity of 8,400 MV.
