2.27      The pavoer plants included in NTPC's investment program form a part
of CEA's long-term least-cost power development program for India. The
majority of NTPC's proposed power stations are pit-head coal fired stations,
often extensions of existing stations, which have relatively low generating
costs. The feasibility studies for stage II developments of Rihand,
Vindhyachal and Unchahar as well as the gas-fired station at Faridabad, were
reviewed and generally found technically and economLcally viable, subject to
the availability of gas for Faridabad. The viability of the other proposed
stations remains to be confirmed upon completion of the feasibility studies,
in particular for the two proposed coastal stations at Kayamkulam and
Mangalore. The investment program vill be reviewed and updated annually,
taking into account NTPC's new investment policies (para. 2.2) and progress
with projects proposed to be executed with private sector partners. The size
of N5PC's investment program would also depend on MTPC's level of internal
resource mobilization and its capability to raise funds from foreign and local
capital markets. Subject to these qualifications the Bank concurs with NTPC's
proposed investment program.
NPM's Future Role in the Indian Power Sector
2.28      The transformation from a major construction oriented organization
to one of the largest generating utilities In the developing world has not
been without problems. With the rapidly growing demand for electricity, the
SBs were keen to obtain power from FMP power stations, but their poor
financial situation limited the ability to pay. Bill collection became such a
problem (para 4.10) that it started to undermine NTPC's financial position to
the extent that some international lenders, including the Bunk, refrained from
providing fresh financial assistance to finace ITPC'8 Investment program,
until collection performance improved. The reality of Indial's continuing
constraints oan public resources requires the mobilization of substantial
amounts of funds from internal resources and a financial strategy much less
dependent on the exchequer than in the past. With the new comuercial policies
and new two part tariff in place, WMP should be able to collect bills and
mobilize sufficient resources from Internal cash generation to finance its
ambitious Investment progrqm.
2.29      In addition, to the implementation of its own investment program,
ETPC is expected to form over the next few years several joint ventures with



- 24 -
the private sector (para. 3.9) and it expects to be responsible for
engineering as mell as operation and maintenance. Although the take-over of
the Unchahar power plant was successfully completed in a relatively short
period of time Cpara.2.8), NTPC does not have immediate plans for taking over
other power stations from the SEBs, partly because of a lack of financial
resources, but also because it would like to first complete the integration of
the Unchahar power station personnel.  At a later stage NTPC might be willing
to consider taking over other SEB power stations.  Through its consulting wing
NTPC is however willing to assist SEBs with the rehabilitation of existing
stations. A dialogue has been initiated by the Bank on what should be NTPC's
future role in the Indian power sector and whether further institutional
changes would be desirable. Another question raised is whether NTPC's
experience in supervising major projects could not be used for improving the
execution of hydro power stations. Such major institutional reforms cannot be
expected to materialize in the short run. Although the dialogue on these
issues will continue the emphasis for NMPC in the short run will be on
commercialization of its operations and building a resource mobilization
strategy which would maximize the use of private resources, possibly through
raising private equity capital.
1II. THE PROJECT
Project Obiectives
3.1       The project has been designed to support 001's reform program in
the power sector. The project objectives are tos
(a) help Improve commercial discipline in the power system through the
Implementation of new comercial and investment policiess
(b) help NTPC meet its targets for capacity additions through Increased
mobilization of funds from internal resources, domestic and foreign
capital markets, as well as from the private sector through joint
venture operations;
(c) upgrade environmental performance of NTPC power stations and make
its new power stations environmentally more sustainable; and
(d) strengthen its environmental and resettlement and rehabilitation
management capability.
Proiect Descri2tion
3.2       The proposed project comprises:
(a) Generation Capacity Additions: a five year time-slice of NTPC's
least cost investment program of new coal and gas-based power
stations for which full funding has not yet been arranged;
(b) Private Sector Component: support to NTPC to undertake several
joint venture operations; and
(c) Environmental Strengthening and Resettlement and Rehabilitation:
implementation of an Environmental Action Plan (RAP) which includes
onvironmental upgrading projects, training and technical assistance



- 25 .
for strengthenfing of NTPC's environmental and RiR management
capability and the implementation of the WA.
Project Components
3.3       Generation Capacity Additions.  NTPC'8 total investment program
(para 2.24) and implementation schedules for each station may change depending
on the availability of fuels (coal and gas), obtaining environmental and other
goverment clearances and availabilLty of financing. During negotiations
agreement has been reached that NTPC furnish to the Bank not later than
December 31 of each year its investment program and corresponding finaneial
projections and financing plan for the Bank's review and comments. Agreement
has also been reached that to be eligible for Bank financing, NTPC new power
plants would have to meet the following criteria:
(a) satisfactory technical, economic and financial viability of the
proposed power plants including fuel and transmission linkages;
(b) all necessary 001 clearances including Public Investment Board
(PIB) and environmental for the power station would have been
obtained; the Environmental Assessments would have to be carried
out in accordance with Bank guidelines and for "A* projects an
Eavironmental Assessment Summary would have to be circulated to the
Board before issuance of tendet documents;
(c) the state in which the plant is to be located would be in
compliance with the terms of existing BPSA;
'd) satisfactory financing plan; and
ie) preparation of a satisfactory Resettlement Action Plan, where
required, duly endorsed by the State authorities.
3.4       Under the proposed approach, NTMC wvuld have the flexibility to use
the proceeds of the Bank loans for power stations even if they are currently
not included in its investment program, provided they meet the eligibility
criteria. The first two power stations in NTPC's investment program for which
Bank finance has been requested are Stage II expansions of the Rihand and
Vindhyachal thermal power plants. For Vindhyachal II, all eligibility
criteria have been met. For Rlhand II also, except for the PIB clearance.
This is expected not later thau September 1993.
3.S       Expansion of the Rihand Thermal Power Plant (Stage II).  The Rihand
TPP is located in the State of Uttar Pradesh on the southern bank of the
Gobind Ballabh Pant Sagar Reservoir (3ihand Reservoir). The ultimate capacity
of the power plant would be 3,000 KW and would include six units of 500 MW
each. Stage I (2x500 NOV was constructed with part financing from the
Government of the United Kingdom and has been in operation since FY91. Stage
III (2xSOO MW) would be built at a later date. The proposed Stage II would
Include two 500 MV coal-fired units involving steam generators, turbo-
generator sets complete with auxiliaries, closed-cycle cooling mater system
with cooling towers, coal transportation, coal, ash and fuel oil handling
plants, a water treatment plant, a 275 meter high chimney, miscellaneous
electrical and mechanical equipment, environmental safeguard and monitoring
equipment, associated civil works and engineering and consulting services.



- 26 -
3.6       Expansion of the Vindhyachal Thermal Power Plant (Stage II).  The
Vindhyachal TPP is located in the State of Madhya Pradesh on the western bank
of the Rihand Reservoir. The ultimate capacity of the power plant would be
2,260 MV. Stage I (6s210 MV) was constructed with part financing from the
Government of the former Soviet Union and has been in operation since FY89.
The proposed Stage II would include two 500 MV coal-fired units involving
equipment components similar to Rihand Stage TI above.
3.7       Fuel Supply and Associated Transmission Lines. Coal and gas for
NTPC's power stations are allocated by 001's Coal and Gas Linkages Committees.
Adequate and timely supply of coal and gas for the new power plants is part of
the eligibility criteria for funding under the proposed loan (para. 3.3).
Rihand II and Vindhyachal II projects would initially be supplied from
Dudhichua Phase IT coal mine in the Singrauli area owned by Northern
Coalfields Ltd. (NCL). The first phase of the Dudhichua mine was developed
with World Bank assistance under Ln. 2353-IN (1984). Environmental clearance
for the mine expansion for a production of up to ten million tons per year was
obtained in July 1990. The coal would be transported to the power plants
through an existing Merry-Go-Round (MGR) system. Coal for Vindhyachal II may
also be supplied at a later stage from the Nigahi mine expansion, also in the
Singrauli area and owned by NCL. The environmental assessment is under
preparation and is scheduled to be submitted for clearance later in 1993.
3.8       The switchyards for Rihsnd II and Vindhyachal II power stations and
associated transmission systems will be constructed by POWERGRID and are
included in its investment program. POWERGRID has obtained forest clearance
for the Rihand to Kanpur 400 kv line. For the Kanpur to Ballabhgarh 400 kv
line no forest land is involved. A detailed survey of the 50 km Rihand to
Singrauli 400 kv line is being conducted. In case forest land cannot be
avoided. the necessary forest clearance will be obtained from MOEF. Forest
clearances for Vindhyachal to Bina 800 kv line and Korba to Bhilai 400 kv line
have been obtained and no forest land is Involved on the Bina-Nagda-Ranason
400 kv line. The substation sites will not displace any homesteads. As
required by MOE?, compensatory forestry will be carried out whenever forest
land is involved. During negotiations, 001 confirmed that adequate and timely
supplies of fuel and financing for the associated transmission systems for
Rihantd II and Vindhyachal II will be made available and that adequate measures
have been taken to ensure the coordination of construction schedules.
3.9       Private Sector Component.  In January 1993, GOI has given NTPC
approval to enter into joint ventures with local and foreign private partners
to Implement projects out of its own investment program and to bid for and
implement projects proposed by SEBs to the private sector. NTPC has signed
MOUs with several potential foreign investors to participate in bids invited
by the states for generating stations and/or is in advance stages of
negotiations for implementation of such projects. NTPC would be a minority
equity partner, but expects to be responsible for the engineering and
operation and maintenance of the power plants. Details for at least two such
projects are expected to be finalized over the next 6 months and a provision
has beean made under the loan so that part of the proceeds of the loan can be
used for financing NTPC's contribution in the venture. The proceeds of the
loan would finance goods and services procured in accordance with the Bank
guidelines and counted as NTPC's contribution to the project. During
negotiations agreement has been reached that to be eligible for Bank financing
the joint venture projects would have to meet the same eligibility criteria as



- 27 -
for NTPC's own projects, suitably modified vith respect to Government
clearances and approvals and the signing of a Power Purchase Agreement (PPA)
satisfactory to the Bank.
3.10      Environmental Strengthening and Resettlement and Rehabilitation.
Implementation of a comprehensive RAP is a significant component of the
proposed loan. The objectives of the ZAP ares (i) to rationalize and improve
NTPC's organizational structure for environmental management at the corporate
and plant levels; (ii) to improve environmental monitoring and the preparation
of environmental impact assessments; (iii) to execute a specific program of
remedial environmental measures for NTPC's existing power stations and make
its operations environmentally more sustainable; and (iv) to address
outstanding R&R issues in existing stations and to upgrade NTPC's capability
to prepare and execute R&R programs. The EAP covers all NTPC power stations
and addresses the required institutional strengthening of NTPC to deal
effectively with environmental and R&R problems. It includes investments
required to implement remedial actions to enable existing plants to meet the
latest GOI environmental standards. The EAP also includes training of NTPC
staff and technical assistance to help undertake studies and implement
environmental projects. Further details of the RAP are provided in paras.3.29
to 3.34 and in Annex 3.2.

3.12      NTPC's Investment requirements including interest during
construction (IDC) during the FY93-FY97 (Eighth Plan) period amount to Re. 177
billion (US$4,960 million equivalent) and Rs. 380 billion (US$9,039 million)
during the Ninth plan period. The investment program includes capital
expenditures for the implementation of the RAP. MTPC's proposed investment
program was discussed and agreed during negotiations. The actual composition
of NTPC's ten year investment program would depend on the project feasibility
studies and the necessary GO0 clearances (para 2.28) and availability of
financial resources. NTPC's overall resource mobilization strategy including
the possible use of an Expanded Cofinancing Operation (ECO) to supplement the
Bank's assistance is further discussed in pars 4.17.
Basis for Cost Estimates
3.13      Cost estimtes for the power stations included in the program are
based on available cost data from NTPC's own stations and those recently built
by the SZBs. Base prices were updated to March 1993. Physical contingencies
of St on equipment and materials and 10? on civil works were assumed on the
basis of NTPC's experience. Price contingencies are based on the expected
annual domestic and International Inflation rates. Moreover, uncertainties
arising from 00I's recent decisions to allow Indian suppliers to bid in
foreign currencies for procurement under ICB, to decrease customs duties and
to rescind some of the *deemed export benefits' have been reflected in the
price contingencies. The domestic inflation rates used for the cost estimates
are as follows: 8.02 for FY94, 7.02 for PY95, 6.OZ for FY6, 5.5? for FY97,
and 5.02 thereafter. The international inflation rates used are 3.1 for FY94
and thereafter. Bank projected US dollar/Rupee exchange rates were used for
currency conversion purposes. Detailed cost estimates for Vindhyachal II and
Rhaud II thermal power plants are given in Annex 3.3.

Lendint Arrangements and Security Arrangements
3.14      The proposed loan of US$400 million is the first of a series of
proposed three operations to support NTPC's investment program which would be
presented for consideration of the Board at intervals of 18 to 24 months for a
total of about US$1.2 billion, provided that WMPC remains In compliance with
the Bank's conditions. The total amount is expected to be disbursed over a
maximum period of five years. The three Bank loans would covers (i) about
8.86 of the total financing requirements of NTPC's ten-year investment
program; and (Ui) about 192 of NTPC's financing requirements for its
investments during the PY93-FY97 period.
3.15      The proposed Loan would be made directly to NTPC with a 001
guarantee, at the Bank's standard variable interest rate, a 20 year maturity
including a five years grace period. Go! will charge a guarantee fee of 12
p.a. on the outstanding amount of the proposed Loan. NTPC would bear the
interest and foreign exchange risks. NTPC has created liens on some of its
assets, including floatation of a series of bonds in the domestic market. They
also have Negative Pledge provisions in their external borrowings. During
negotiations, agreement has been reached that NTPC will create an equitable
mortgage in favor of the Bank on its unencumbered assets by way of security
for the proposed loan by December 31, 1993. The Bank will have no objection
if other external lenders want to share the mortgage with the Bank on a pari
passu basis.
Generation Capacity Additions
3.16      NPC's Engineering and Proiect Preparation Capability.  NTPC would
be responsible for implementing all the physical components of the proposed
project.  In the course of implementing previous Bank and non-Bank financed
power generation projects, NTPC has developed, with the assistance of several
international consultants, adequate capability and expertise on the
mngine-ing of thermal power plants equipped with 2001210 NW and 500 MV coal-
fired units, and gas based combined cycle power plants. NTPC designs and
manages its projects with little use of outside consultants and while
suppliers are generally responaible for erection works, NMPC retains the
responsibility for Iaplementatiou coordination.
3.17      Status of Engineering and Project Preparation.  The general
characteristics and layout for the coal-fired and gas-based combined cycle
power plants would be similar to those of existing TPC projects. The
feasibility reports for the projects included in the early phases of NTPC's
investment program have beer. prepared by NTPC. Those for Rlhand UI and
Vindhyachal II were reviewed by the Bank and found acceptable.  Design
reports, draft tender documents and tecbnical specifications for the boiler
and turbine-generator contract packages for Rihand SI and Vindhyachal II were
prepared by NTPC and agreed to by the Bank. Specifications for the other
contract packages for Rihand II and Vindhyachal It, and for packages for the
other power plant projects becoming eligible for financing under the proposed
loan would also be prepared by NTPC.
3.18      Status of Clearances. NTPC has obtained all GOI clearances
required for Rihand Il and Vindhyachal II, except for the Public Investment
Board clearance for Ruhand II, which is expected not later than September
1993.



- 30 -
3.19      Prolect Implementation and Mana&ement.  NTPC has adequate
experience and organizational capacity in managing simultaneously the
construction of large power stations which it has demonstrated during the
construction of the previous projects. In late 1980s NTPC managed the
construction of eleven power plant projects semultaneously. Headquarters
support the design, procurement, contracting and financial operations. Day-
to-day implementation is undertaken at the sites headed by a General Manager,
with close monitoring from the senior management. NTPC's project management
structure for typical coal-fired power plant projects are shown in Annex 3.4.
The site General Managers chair monthly review meetings attended by project
staff and senior executives of concerned headquarter departments to review
progress and authorize payments to suppliers an#; erection contractors. The
site General MNangers report through the field organization to the Executive
Directors of their respective regions. NTPC senior management receives
monthly progress reports and reviews each project in detail in quarterly
Management Committee meetings. Additional management-level meetings are also
convened at short notice when needed to resolve urgent issues in project
implementation. NTPC is upgrading its computerized project management systems
to help schedule and monitor project implementation and facilitate
comprehensive and timely progress reporting.
3.20      The power plant projects eligible for funding under the proposed
loan would be implemented in line with NTPC's standard project management
philosophy. Suppliers would, in general, provide the final designs and would
supply and erect the major equipment (boilers, turbine-generators, coal and
ash handling plants, cooling water system, control and instrumentation
systems, etc.). NTPC staff would be responsible for the approval of final
manufacturing and construction drawings, would supervise and monitor all
tests, oversee the plant construction and be responsible for coordinating the
procurement and erection activities.
3.21      NTPC has over the years, built-up adequate engineering capability
(para. 3.16), and it does not employ independent consultant-engineers to
supervise the construction of its conventional power plant projects.
3.22      Project Implementation.  The detailed implementation schedules for
Rihand II and Vindhyachal II are given in Annex 3.5.  The bid documents for
the boilers and turbine-generators of Vindhyachal II would be issued in June
1993, bids would be opened in September 1993, the contracts would be awarded
in March 1994. This timetable would mean that the first 500 MW unit would be
synchronized with the transmission system by March 1999. These units would
then be commissioned (put in commercial operation) by September 1999. The
second 500 MW unit wvould be synchronized and commissioned in March 2000 and
September 2000. The units of Rihand II would be commissioned about three
months later. Annex 3.5 also includes the overall implementation schedules of
other projects included in NTPC's investment program and key milestone data.
Procurement
3.23      Procurement Philosophy and Bidding Documents.  As in previous
projects it implemented, NTPC designed its contract packages with due
consideration for simplifying project management and encourage as many
competent manufacturers and contractors as possible to bid by appropriately
packing the various contracts. Major power station equipment would be



- 31 -
procured on a supply and erect basis in accordance with a bidding document for
Supply-cum-Zrection of Goods, which has been agreed between the Bank and NTPC.
This document would be used until the Bank's standard bidding document for
supply and erection of goods is issued. For procurement of all other Bank
financed contract packages, including those for the RAP, the Bank's standard
bidding documents for civil works, supply of goods and supply-cum-supervision
of erection would be used.
3.24      ITPC's Procurement Strately.  NTPC intends to procure most of the
major contract packages of the power plant projects eligible for Bank funding
under international competitive bidding (ICB) procedures, in accordance with
Bank guidelines. In selected cases, NTPC will use Bank funds only for partial
payments of the ICB contracts; the balance would be paid from export credits,
other commercial sources, bonds or STPC's own resources. Other contract
packages will be financed directly through export credits. It is expected that
contracts for large equipment packages such as boilers, turbine-generators,
electrostatic precipitators, transformers, etc. would be attractive to
offshore official and commercial lenders with an interest in supporting their
client contractors. This would be agreed between the Bank and NTPC during the
finalization of the financing plan for each particular power plant. In such
cases, NTPC would specify in the tender documents that foreign exchange
financing is sought at OECD consensus terms or better. Non-provision of such
financing would be cause for rejection of the bids under this scheme; and the
criteria to be used for evaluating the financial offers would be clearly given
in the tender documents. Contract packages funded by other bilateral or
multilateral donors would be procured in accordance with their respective
guidelines.
3.25      Procurement Arrangements under the proposed loan. The procurement
arrangements and packaging for Rihand II and Vindhyachal II are summarized and
msjor milestones of the procurement process are given in Annex 3.6. The major
part of the contract packages for Rihand II and Vindhyachal II (totalling
about US$1.5 billion and including all Bank financed items) as well as Bank
financed packages of the RAP would be procured through ICB, in accordance with
the Bank's guidelines and following the overall NTPC procurement strategy
outlined in paras 3.23 and 3.24 above. NTPC has also requested that part of
environmental projects under the ZAP be financed from savings under Lu. 2844-
IN National Capital Power Supply and Ln. 2845-IN Talcher Thermal and it is
proposed that the legal agreements be suitably amended. Under the proposed
loan, procurement under ICB is expected to total US$374 million or 94Z of the
loan amount. The following additional specific procedures vould apply to all
procurement under the proposed loans (i) all contracts for materials and
equipment or for erection services financed under the proposed loan and
estimated to cost US$300,000 equivalent or more would be procured through ICB,
in accordance with the Bank's procurement guidelines and using standard
bidding documents; (Ui) all contracts for materials and equipment financed
under the proposed loan and expected to cost less than US$300,000 equivalent
and not exceeding in the aggregate US$5 million equivalent, may be procured
under Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) procedures satisfactory to the Bank;
(iii) civil works contracts would only f- financed by the Bank if it is
included in a Bank financed equipment p-;age and essential for functional
purposes; and (iv) specialized equipment. qich as environmental monitoring
instruments and control equipment costing less than US$100,000 may be procured
by inviting quotations from at least three suppliers and items of a
proprietary in nature may be procured directly from suppliers, provided the



- 32 -
aggregate amount does not exceed US$20 million equivalent. NTPC would furnish
In advance lists of proprietary items to be procured directly for review and
approval by the Bank.
S3.26     Local manufacturers would be expected to bid for materials and
equipment packages, and a domestic preference of 15X or the corresponding
import duty, whichever is less, would be applied in the comparison of bids
submitted by local manufacturers for materials and equipment contracts. Prior
Bank review of bid documents and approval of contract awards would be
mandatory for all contracts expected to cost the equivalent of US$5 million or
more. Smaller Bank-financed contracts under ICB would be subject to selective
post-award review, but the aggregate value of such contracts is not expected
to exceed US$30 million. Bank financed consultants would be appointed in
accordance with the Guidelines for the Selection of Consultants. Consultancy
contracts estimated at US$100,000 or more would be subject to prior review.
3.27      Procurement under further Bank loans. The proposed loan would be
the first of the series of three Bank loans to NTPC that would be proposed to
support NTPC'8 investment program provided that NTPC remains in compliance
with the agreed loan conditions. It is anticipated that part of the proceeds
of the second and third Bank loans would be used to finance the balance
portion of payments for contracts earlier awarded in accordance with Bank
procurement guidelines under the proposed loan.
Disbursements
3.28       Disbursements under the proposed loan would be made against for up
tot (a) 1002 of the foreign currency expenditures (CIF), 1OOX of the
ex-factory cost and 802 of local expenditures for other items procured
locally; (b) 702 of expenditures for civil works and erection services; and
(c) 1OOZ of the services rendered by consultants and for training.
Disbursements fors (a) equipment contracts valued less than US$300,000
equivalent; and (b) civil works and erection contracts valued less than
US$500,000 would be made against statements of expenditures (SON), the
documentation of which would not be sent to the Bank, but would be retained by
NTPC for inspection by supervision missions. All other disbursements would be
fully documented. To facilitate disbursements, a special account would be
established for the Bank loan with an authorized allocation of US$25 million,
equivalent to four months of average disbursements under the proposed Loan.
Annex 3.7 shows the estimated disbursement schedule as derived from the
construction programs of the project components, assuming normal terms for
commercial payments, including use of lank guarantees for retention payments.
The disbursement profile for the loan does not fit IBRD's standard profiles
because of the time-slice nature of the operation. The closing date for the
loan wmuld be September 30, 1997. Expenditures for materials, equipment and
services for the Environmental Strengthening component, incurred by NTPC
within 12 months prior to the sigLAng of the loan agreement, under contracts
resulting from advance actions by NTPC, procured in accordaice with Bank
guidelines and awarded with prior Bank approval, would be eligible for
retroactive financing under the proposed loan up to an aggregate amount of
US$10 million.

Environmental Aspects
3.29      Under existlng 001 procedures NTPC has to prepare a detailed
Envaironmental Impact Assessment (EIA) in order to obtain an environmental
clearance from the Ministry of Environment and forests (OEF). Further
details of the current environmental regulatory framework pertaining to NTPC
and a description of pertinent GOI and World Bank standards is given in Annez
2.4. The ZIA required by M40F is in substance and form similar to
Environmental Assessments required by the Bank for category A projects and
full Environmental Assessments have been carried out for Rihand II and
Vindhyachal II the first projects to be taken up under the project. The
designs of these stations are compatible with both 001 and World Bank
standards and the Environmental Assessment Summaries (EAS) were distributed to
the Board on April 14, 1993. A brief summary of both EAS' and an overview of
the specific environmental issues pertaining to the Singrauli area is given In
Annex 3.8. Agreement has been reached with NTPC that for all subsequent Bank
financed power plants under the project, category A environmental assessments
would be completed and reviewed with the Bank before issuance of tender
documents for Bank financed procurement packages. An EAS would be circulated
to the Board.
3.30      The Environmental Action Plan. The RAP can be divided into four
main categoriess (i) Institutional strengthening; (1i) Eavironmental
management and monitoring; (Wi) Environmental projects and;
Civ) Rehabilitation and Resettlement (para 3.33). Implementation of the RAP
has already started (Annex 3.2) and the remaining actions will also be
undertaken largely by NTPC's own personnel, but with the help of consultants.
Implementation of the ZAP will be the responsibility of the respective
environmental groups namely lEG, EMG and CDG at the Corporate Headquarters and
plant levels. The progress of the overall lmplementation of ZAP will be
monitored by the Corporate Planning Department. Technical assistance will be
provided by consultants in areas like review of existing environmental
policies and standards, training and a number of specific technical studies to
design further remedial actions. The detailed implementation schedule of the
ZAP is shown in Annex 3.2, most of the actions are expected to be completed by
the end of 1996.  Implementation of the RAP dated May 10, 1993 agreed with
the Bank during negotiations is a condition of the proposed loan.
3.31      The consultants arrangements for the implementation of the RAP are
as follows. NTPC already has a twinning arrangement with British Electricity
International Ltd. (BRI) of United Kingdom and experts have visited the NTPC
facilities to assess their needs. Under this arrangement SRI will continue to
assist NTPC in (a) conducting environmental appraisal; (b) developing
guidelines for environmental management; and (c) exa;a1ning current laboratory
and monitoring practices and recommending additional requirements.
3.32      I  addition the E-7 network, a group of internationally reputed
utilities !1 have agreed in principle to provide assistance to NTPC for the
lmplementation of the RAP. The assistance will be by way of grant of about 50
man-months time of their experts and cover the cost of visits of NTPC
6/ The group consist of seven major utilities: Electricitd de France,
Hydro-Quebec, Ontario iydro, Tokyo Electric Power Co., Kensai Electric Power
Co., BRUAG and EMNL.



- 34 -
personnel to their facilities. These major utilities are well versed with
international practices, govenwmental regulations and would have a pragmatic
view on environmental matters concerning large coal based power projects.
Under this program, NTPC's expertise will be developed in (a) developing
overall targets for pollution and waste reduction (including energy
conservation); (b) development and engineering of effluent treatment and
emission control systems; (c) power stations operations from environmental
view point and utilizing of ash; (d) establishing computer center and data

given in Annex 3.2.
Resettlement and Rehabilitation
3.33      NTPC has revised its R&R policy (para 2.13) and all R&R related to
the construction of its new power stations will be handled in accordance with
the new policy. The institutional strengthening of NTPC and manpower
development to effectively deal with RUR issues forms an integral part of the
RAP. As part of its new RR policy NTPC will also carry out socio-economic
surveys to ascertain the present economic status of project affected families
(PAls) and provides, where found to be needed, for the funding and
implementation of community development programs, including necessary income
restoration measures. More specifically, during negotiations agreement was
reached that NTPC would undertake such studies not later than ecember 31,
1994 for PAFs of the Second Parakka Thermal Power project (Lu. 2442-IN, 1984),
the Combined Cycle Project (Ln. 2674-IN, 1986) and the National Capital Power
Project (Ln. 2844-IN, 1987). Based on these surveys NTPC would draw up
whenever necessary, remedial action plans In consultation with the Bank which
have as their objective the phased restoration of the economic base of the
persons affected. Under the Talcher Thermal Power Project (Lu. 2895-IN, 1987),
a Rehabilitation Action Plan, satisfactory to the Bank, is already under
iplementation.
3.34      NTPC has prepared Resettlement Action Plans (RAPs) satisfactory to
the Bank for Rihand II (67 PAFs) and Vindhyachal II (119 PAPs) in consultation
with the local authorities and people concerned. The draft RAPs were
discussed during appraisal and they have been further revised to bring them in
accordance with NTPC's revised R&R policy. Implementation of the agreed RAPs
is a condition of the loan. During negotiations, an understanding was reached
that NTPC will review with the Bank on an annual basis: (a) the application of
the revised RUR policy; (b) implementation of the ongoing RAPs in Bank
financed thermal power projects and other R&R aspects under the RAP as agreed
with the Bank. The 87 PAFs of the Stage 1I of the Dudhichua coal mine are
being resettled in accordance with the Integrated Environmental Management
Plan (EMP) for the NCL coal fields approved by MOEF in 1987. For subsequent
power plants to be financed by the Bank under the project, the preparation of
a Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) satisfactory to the Bank and where required,
endorsed by the State authorities is one of the eligibility criteria (3.3).



- 35 -
Proiect Monitoring and Implementation Review
3.35      Under ongoing Bank financed projects NTPC is required to prepare
quarterly reports covering the work of consultants, procurement, physical
progress, project costs, disbursements, administrative and financial aspects
of the project. Progress reports under the proposed project will be prepared
along the same lines, but their scope will be expanded to: (a) cover NTPC's
overall financial and institutional performance; (b) progress in the
implementation of the RAP; and (c) present key quantitative measures of
implementation performance for power stations financed by the Bank. Progress
reports will be sent to the Bank within four weeks of the conclusion of the
reporting period; the first report is to be submitted for the period ending
December 1993. Because of its nature the project will require more than
regular Bank supervision time and is estimated at about 30 staff weeks per
year. Staff inputs for the preparation of the second and third loans should,
however, be lower than the current norms. The BSank's project supervision plan
is outlined in Annex 3.9.
3.36      Mid-Term review.  The proposed operation is expected to be
completed within two to three years. Therefore, a mid-term review would not
be required. However, the following issues would be revisited during the
appraisal of the second and third Bank loan for supporting NTPC's investment
program: (i) status of GOI's reform program in the power sector and NTPC's
role in it; (ii) overall progress in the physical execution of the projects
funded under the proposed loan; (iiI) review of the adequacy of the level of
self financing agreed under this loan; and (iv) compliance with legal
covenants, including satisfactory implementation of the RAP, including
performance on R&R.
IV. FINANCE
Financial Management
4.1       Organization.  All financial and commercial functions of NTPC are
under the control of the Director (Finance) who is assisted by an Executive
Director and two general managers. Finance staff at regional 6ffices, power
stations and projects report administratively to the executive directors and
general managers of projects, but functionally to finance staff at corporate
headquarters. The regional Heads of Finance are responsible for billing and
collection of dues from the SEBs and for the coordination between finance
staff at the project sites and corporate headquarters.
4.2       Because of the lack of adequate facilities in India, NTPC has built
up an efficient communication network between various remote project sites,
the regional headquarters and the Corporate Offices at Delhi. The Corporation
was amongst the first in India to lease channels of the communication
satellite, INSAT, to link the headquarters to its sites. Augmented by radio
frequency wireless, the Corporation has voice, facsimile and computer links to
its stations and regional offices. These communication linkages have
facilitated development of an advanced Management Information System and being
used to exercise operational and financial control.



- 36 -
4.3       Accounting and Budgeting.  NTPC keeps its accounts in accordance
with the commercial accounting systems defined in the Companies Act, taking
Into account the relevant provisions of the Electricity (supply) Act, 1948.
NTPC's accounting system was originally designed in 1979 by M/s A.F. Fergusons
and Company, Chartered Accountants, it has kept pace with the rapid growth of
the corporation. It has well conceived computerized systems for financial and
cost accounting, project accounting, inventory control, billing. fized assets
accounting, and payroll. The project cost accounting system is now being
supplemented by cost accounting for operations. Computerized financial
accounting systems have been Implemented at all sites. NTPC has developed
budgetary control systems for capital investment and operation and
maintenance. Its accounting and budgeting systems have been effective tools
for planning, tariff calculations. monitoring and control of expenditures at
all levels of the organization and has raised cost awareness amongst its
managers. Current cost accounting, including revaluation of assets, is not
prescribed by GOT, but in order to obtain more accurate information about its
actual financial performance and to assess the Impact of inflation, NTPC has
been preparing since FY88 summarized current cost accounts to measure the
Impact of inflation and the current costs of NTPC historically valued assets.
Most financial management systems were developed by NTPC's own staff.
Treasury management both in local and foreign currencies requires now more
attention, because of the situation in the capital markets and increased
foreign exchange exposure. It was therefore agreed that a review of NTPC's
financial management systems be undertaken within the framework of its
twinning arrangement with BRI, UK. At negotiations agreement has been reached
that NTPC submit to the Bank not later than December 31, 1993 the scope of the
financial management review and to discuss with the Bank not later thAn June
30, 1994 the implementation of the recommendations.
4.4       Audit. The Internal Audit function is under General Manager,
Finance, who reports to the Director of Finance. In addition, there are audit
cells at project sites. To supplement NTPC's in house capabilities, audit
firms have been engaged to undertake internal audit of selected projects. The
Internal audit covers financial transactions, works and accounts, contracts,
and review of operations and systems of functional departments. As per the
Companies Act, NTPC accounts are audited annually by a group of private fimss
of chartered accountants appointed (generally for a three year period). In
addition, the Comptroller & Auditor General of India (CAG) carries out an
annual audit which is more limited in its scope. The audit arrangements are
satisfactory. Agreement has been reached at negotiations that NTPC will
submit annually to the Bank, not later than seven months after the close of
the fiscal year, its audited financial statements, together with the audit
report from the private auditors and the comments of the CAG, as well as the
audit reports of the special account and withdrawals made against statement of
expenditures for the project.
4.5       Insurance.  NTPC follows sound insurance practices, generally
consistent with power utilities practice. Goods in transit from overseas to
India are insured, as are both projects under construction (except in the case
of turnkey contracts, where the contractor is required to take proper
coverage) and units in operation.
4.6       Taxes. With regard to income taxes, NTPC is required to file
returns following the norms established by the Companies Act and the Income
Tax Act. Taxes are payable at the rate of 45Z of the net taxable income. In



- 37 -
addition. a surcharge of 152 is also payable. However, because of its high
level of capital investment and the much higher depreciation rates allowed for
incme tax computation, NTPC has hardly paid any income tax so far. IT
addition, both NTPC and the SEBs are exempt from a norm that requires tax
payment whenever book profits are shown. In regard to customs duties, NTPC is
subject to a reduced rate of 20? on most imports related to its projects. No
excise taxes are levied on bulk supplies of electricity, but NTPC has to
reimburse manufacturers the excise taxes paid by them in respect of NTPC's
capital equipment purchased from them. Electricity duties are levied by some
States on the electricity sold by NTPC.
4.7       Financial Planning. NTPC two year rolling budgetary systems for
capital investment and for revenues. Their preparation begins in
SeptemberlOctober of each year with an update of the current year budgets.
The revenue budget is used for determining profitability, working capital
requirements and internal cash generation. In addition to its yearly budgets,
NTPC prepares five year investment plans for the Planning Commission, which
are also updated annually. NTPC's corporate planning department in
cooperation with other units within the corporation, has developed a
computerized model for long term financial projections, which is being used to
analyze long term financing needs and for sensitivity analyses such as size
and composition of the investment programs, inflation, foreign exchange
fluctuations etc. The model has been used for the preparation of this project
and is being applied as a tool for management decision making.

per annum and revenues with 36Z per annum. In FY92, NTPC revenues about 45S
to the Northern region, 32Z to the Western region, 18Z to the Southern region
and the balance (5Z) in the Eastern Region.

Billing and Collection
4.9       Monthly bills for energy drawn by the SEBs of the four regions are
prepared at the Commercial Department within about two weeks of the month
following the consumption. Depending on the facilities available and co-
ordination among the beneficiaries, three to fifteen days are required in
different regions to compile the simultaneous meter readings with
representatives of the SEBs involved and to send the accounts to headquarters.
The process includes the compilation of inter-system exchanges and preparation
of regional energy accounts by the Regional Electricity Boards. (REBBs). Bills
are due for payment 30 days after issuance. If the bills are paid through a
letter of credit upon presentation of the bills, a rebate of 2.52 of the
amount paid through LC is granted. NTPC has had difficulties in concluding
Bulk Power Supply Agreements (BPSAs) with the SE8s. During the period 1983 to
1985 four BPSAs were signed with the SEBs for Singrauli, Korba, Ramagundam and
Farakka power stations, but for most stations commissioned after 1985, bills
were issued at ad hoc rates, without having a transparent contractual
framework in place. Following the notification of the new two part tariffs
(para 2.2), bills are now issued on that basis and NTPC is in the process of
concluding new BPSAs for each region which will cover both coal and gas based
power plants. A draft BPSA was reviewed during appraisal and found
satisfactory. To help NTPC in concluding the BPSA's, the Ministry of Power
has issued an advisory to inform all NTPC's clients that NTPC's new commercial
policies (para 2.2) will be applicable to all supplies of power by NTPC.
This includes NTPC's right to cut off power to defaulting clients. One
regional BPSA (for the Eastern Region) was recently signed. During
negotiations, agreement has been reached that two more regional BPSA be signed
by December 31, 1993 and the last one be concluded not later than March 31,
1994.
4.10      Bill collection has been a persistent problem for NTPC, because of
the poor financial situation of many SEBs and the lack of an appropriate
contractual framework. (See graph) A covenant specifying the level of accounts
receivable not to exceed an amount equivalent to the proceeds of its sales of
power for the two preceding months, was first introduced for NTPC under Ln
2555-IN Rihand Power Transmission Project approved in May 1985 with effect
from the end of fiscal year 198511986. The covenant was repeated in three
subsequent NTPC Bank loans, but NTPC has never been able to comply. At the
end of FY91 NTPC's level of account receivable was equivalent to 5.4 months of
sales. In June 1990, 001 took the drastic measure of introducing Central
Appropriations (CA) to recover an amount of Rs. 10,205 million (about US$340
million at current exchange rates) of arrears accumulated as of the end of May
31, 1990 over a period of four years. In October 1992, 600 agreed to recover
an additional amount of Rs. 7,500 million of arrears through CA over a period
of four years. In February 1992, NTPC acquired the Unchahar power station in
lieu of arrears (about Rs. 4,800 million, para 2.8) and combined with other
bill collection efforts, NTPC was able to considerably reduce its level of
accounts receivable despite the rapid increase in sales. In PY92, it realized
about 792 of total billing in cash from the SEBes, but combined with the CA and
the take over of Unchabar, bill realization rose to 992 and the level of
accounts receivable came down to 3.4 months of sales equivalent. In FY93,
NTPC realized 83Z of billing in cash from the SnEB and 91Z including payments
made by GOI through CA. At the end of FY93, the overall level of accounts
receivable was 3.3 months of sales equivalent, but excluding the amount still
to be paid through CA, it is 1.4 months of sales equivalent. More encouraging
is that during the last three months of FY93, 93Z of billing was realized



- 39 -
directly from the SEB8. The new comercial policies and lPSA. should enable
D5PC to reach a level of bill collection close to 1002 during FY94. In
several states 100? realizatLon was already achieved In PY93. During
negotiations agreement has been reached that NTPC maintains its accounts
receivable (excluding the amounts still due from Central Appropriations (CA))
at a level not exceeding an amount equivalent to the preceding two months
billing and G00 has given an undertaking to pay to NTPC the remaining CA
during PY94 (Rs. 4,582 million), PY95 (Rs. 1,999 million) and FY96 (R8.1,999
million).

4.12      NTIC's operating data reflect the growth the Corporation
experienced. Net fixed assets in operation increased more than fourfold since
1988, mostly because of continuing capacity additions (para 2.25), but also
because of the take over of the 420 M Ulnchahar power station (para 2.8). The
continuing growth of the level of work-in-progress reflects the NTPC's large
expansion program. NTPC's cost plus tariffs combined with high operating
efficiency and an automatic fuel adjustment clause have enabled it to achieve
relatively high rates of return on net fixed assets In operation achieved
(well in excess of the 9.5Z required under the Bank loans, starting from
1990/91). Its debt service coverage remained comfortable during the whole
period, but it should be noted that debt repayments only started in FY89 as a
result of the relatively favorable terms at which 001 used to onlend
multilateral and bilateral funds in the early stages of NTPC'e development.
The relatively high level of self financing ratio in PY92 is however somewhat
misleading, because it largely reflects an accounting change (essentially a
shift from current to fixed assets) resulting from the take over of the
Unchahar power station. Working capital requirements declined, but it did not
ease NTPC's liquidity problems as it did not provide any cash. Excluding the
effects of the Unchabar power station, NTPC's net contribution to its FY92
Investment program would be about 20?.

4.13      As shown in Table 4.2, NTPC financed more than half of its rapid
growth by borrowing.   Over the last 10 years, 001 financed a total of Rs. 63
billion, -- or about t32 of NTPC's total capital investment requirements, --
through equity contr jutions; the balance from loans and bonds. About 302 of
all loans raised were loans made to C00  by multilateral and bilateral donors
and onlent to NTPC. This denotes a heavy reliance on G00 support, which
during NTPC's initial period of growth was a sound policy. The Bank including
IDA provided almost US$ 3 billion equivalent towards NTPC's total financing
requirements over the past ten years. Initially the Bank assistance was
partly passed on  z equity and partly as loans, but all recent Bank loans were
onlent in full. 60I's loans generally have a total maturity of 20 years,
including 5 years of grace and an interest rate which has changed over time
and but currently is 16? p.a. NTPC also floated bonds in the domestic market
for a total of about Rs. 28 billion, mostly through private placements. In
addition, NTPC raised funds in Japan through a syndicate of banks and
financial institutions and has obtained credits through European suppliers
associated with the construction of Its power stations and the HVDC
transmission line. Over the years NTPC received financing directly or through
001 from the OPEC fund, the Saudi Fund, the governments of Germany, the United
Kingdom, the Soviet Union, Sweden, France, Belgium, Norway and Italy and
untied funds from Japan.
NTPC's Current Financial Position
4.14      At the end of FY92, NTPC still had a relatively solid financial
position. Its long-term debt was only 48? of its capital structure, in
striking contrast with the position of most SEBs In the country, and well
below the typical financial leverage -of 60? to 80X- found in most power
utilities around the world. NTPC's exposure to foreign exchange fluctuations
has been Increasing and at the end of FY92, about 45? of its long term debt
was denominated in foreigu currencies. Increases in fuel can be automatically
passed on to NTPC's clients, but NTPC was, before the introduction of the new
two part tariff, not automatically protected against foreign exchange
fluctuations nor was it able to fully recover Increased operating cost.
Despite this, the return on net average fixed assets In operation in FY92 was



- 41 -
about l52 , while the debt service coverage was 2.4. NTPC's liquidity
problems have been increasing, not only because of its bill collection
problems explained above and the additional working capital requirements for
Unchahar, but also because Coal India Ltd started its cash and carry policy.
Increasingly NTPC has had to make use of cash credits to tie over short-ter
liquidity problems. Following the transfer of transmission assets to
POWERGRID (para 2.6), with effect from April 1, 1992, NTPC's assets and
labilities were reduced by an estimated value of about Rs. 35 billion. The
transfer, however, has not affected NTPC's financial situation.
4.15      The improvements in bill collection in FY93 have helped NTPC to
ease its liquidity problems, but an additional set back during FY93 was the
adverse situation in the domestic bond market, which prevented NTPC from
raising any bonds during the year. This factor combined with the lack of
fresh financial assistance from the multilateral and bilateral donors forced
NTPC to have to delay the start up of new projects. This explains the
constderable drop in the size of NTPC's investment program during the period
FY93-95 as many projects started in the 80's are now close to completion.

4.17      The composition of the NTPC's Investment program is discussed in
paras. 2.25 to 2.29. The financing plan takes into account all loans already
arranged for ongoing projects and the proposed series of three loans and
assumes the full disbursement of the series of three Bank loans over a maximum
of five years. The revenue levels are driven by the assets in couercial
operation and the financial projections assume the full application of the new
two part tariffs. The assumptions are given in Annex 4.3. Under Under the
current part tariffs increases in fuel cost and actual interest cost can be
automatically passed on. As part of the new two part tariff NTPC is also able
to pass on the foreign exchange risk on foreign borrowing, provided CEA
agrees. NTPC's revenues are driven by its asset base and its ability to put
new assets into com nrcial operation. Like in the case most regulated public
utilities in the world, resource mobilization from internal resources is
restricted because of the normative tariff parameters, but NTPC has often be
able to operate at higher efficiency levels. Assuming that NTPC would be able
to collect its bills with the new cojmurcial policies in place the projected
level of internal cash generation can be considered reasonable. The financing
plan assumes GOI equity contributions amounting to US$350 million equivalent
during the Eighth Plan period, which are entirely financed by GOI through
already coumitted loans made directly to GOI by various bilateral donors. No
new equity support has been assumed. The remaining financing gap during the
Zighth Plan, estimated at US$1.4 billion equivalent is expected to be covered
by domestic bonds and loans from bilateral sources, export credits and other
cinmercial borrowings, including a possible EGO from the Bank. OECF has
appraised the Paridabad gas fired power station and NTPC hopes to conclude
negotiations before the end of 1993. NTPC has also plans to raise about
US$100-150 million from private sources, possibly through a private placement
of securities in the international capital markets to help finance their
investment program and it might be able to mobilize more funds from export
credit agencies. Most of NTPC's funding needs will however be from FY1995
onwards and it is considered that the financing gap is manageable.
4.18      Current interest rates in the domestic bond market are relatively
high and NTPC has not been able to raise any bonds in the domestic markets
during FY93. Discussions with local institutional lenders such as LIC, UTI,
IDBI and IPCI about a possible loan are in advance stages. These funds would
be used until such time that the domestic bond market improves.
4.19      With the adoption of the time slice approach and the expected
continuing paucity of GOI resources, NTPC would have to develop a medium and
long term financing strategy to maximize the mobilization of internal and
external resources both from official and private resources including equity.
During negotiations agreement has been reached on the terms of reference of a
medium and long term financial strategy study, which will be discussed with
the Bank not later than September 30, 1994 and thereafter implement the agreed
recommendation. The terms of reference are given in Annex 4.4. Agreement has
also been reached that NTPC would furnish annually by December 31 its
financial projections, including its investment program and financing plan,
for Bank review. In general, the Bank's dialogue with NTPC and potential
lenders on its financing strategy will form an important element of
supervision.

4.21      NTPC's financial position will be monitored by the Bank on the
basis of the annual submissions of the financial projections and its financing
strategy will be regularly discussed with the Bank. With the improved bill
collection NTPC should be able to remain financially sound and be able to
implement its proposed investment program with a substantial contribution to
investment from internal cash generation. At negotiations agreement has been
reached that NTPC shall take all necessary measures, including, adjustments of
tariffs to produce, starting from FY94, funds from internal cash generation
equivalent to not less than 1S5 of its capital expenditures (three year
moving average) and not less than 20Z in each fiscal year thereafter. For the
Ninth Plan period, it is difficult at this stage to make a definite assessment
about the feasibility of the financing plan, but based on past trends and the
projected level of self financing the funding gap could be met. Possibilities
in the international capital markets will be further explored during the
coming months. Only relatively short maturities are currently available in
the domestic bond market. Experience from NTPC and other public enterprises
has shown, however, that these debts can be rolled over at maturity to help
ease cash flow problems. Increased capital and interest cost can be largely
recovered from its customers, but NTPC's liquLdity position remains sensitive
to reductions in bill collection and delays in project implementation as cost
recovery only starts after commissioning. NTPC should also seek to limit its
short and medium-term borrowing, to better match the fixed asset depreciation
permitted under the Act. Sensitivity analyses have demonstrated that these
problems are manageable, as long as NTPC is able to collect its revenues. If
bill collections deteriorate again, NTPC's internal resource mobilization
would be affected and this in turn would force NTPC to delay investments in
new power plants.

V. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
8conomic Analysis
5.1       The analysis focuses on NTPC's core investment program, which is
supported in part by the proposed Bank loan. The two generation projects
which meet the selection criteria for Bank financing and are ready for
implementation under the proposed loan, Vindhyachal and Rihand 2xS00 NV coal-
fired power station extensions, form an integral part of this core investment
program.
5.2       Economic Viability of NTPC's Investment Program.  The economic rate
of return (ERR) was calculated on the basis of the incremental cost and
benefit streams associated with the 1993-1999 time-slice of NTPC's investment
program. Capital costs include the costs of generation facilities
commissioned during the time-slice period.V The investment plan is
discussed in paras. 2.25-28. Financial costs of the program have been
converted to economic terms by excluding taxes and duties and by applying a
standard conversion factor (SCF) of 0.8 to the residual local costs. Economic
cost of NTPC's main fuel (local coal) was updated from the Bank's study of the
Long Tern Issues in the Power Sector (para. 1.1), gas was priced at fuel oil
parity. The prices NTPC pays for its coal supplies are generally slightly
higher than the estimated economic costs, the price of gas is about 75Z of
fuel oil parity. Other operating and maintenance (O&M) costs were taken from
estimates prepared for NTPC's financial projections and converted into
economic terms by applying the SCP. NTPC's incremental tariff revenue was
used as a proxy for economic benefits. NTPC's tariffs are discussed In paras.
2.21-23, billing and collection In paras. 4.09-4.10. Tariff revenue is
regarded to be the minimum measure of the actual benefits as tariff revenue
reflects only a portion of the total benefits of NTPC's operations (paras.
5.6-5.7)g yet the ERR of the program is satisfactory at about 14.72 with this
conservative revenue-based benefit valuation (Annex 5.1).
5.3       Robustness of the Program.  NTPC's time-slice ERR is not
sigr.ificantly affected by changes in the costs of the investment program and
in the implementation schedules of its individual components. This is due to
NTPC's ability under its tariff setting principles to fally pass on to its
clients its actual investment and fuel costs and these account for the major
part of NTPC's total costs. The tariff includes a provision for recovering
operating and maintenance (0&M) costs using normative tariff parameters, i.e.
not on the basis of actual costs. The impact of adverse changes from the most
critical normative values was examined in sensitivity analysis. A 1O0
increase in heat rate (reduction in efficiency) brought the ERR down to 13.42;
a 10? reduction in plant load factor (PLF) reduced ERR to 12.3?. Should both
of these adverse changes take place simultaneously, the ERR is reduced further
to 11.0?. It should be noted that NTPC may also be able to exceed the
normative performance (heat rate, availability etc.) targets, in which case
the ERRs would correspondingly increase from the base case EM.
7/ Associated transmission investments will undertaken by POVERGRID. The
economic viability of PONERGRID's investment program, including transmission
associated with NTPC's power stations, has been established under Ln. 3577-IN
approved on March 23, 1993.



- 45 -
5.4       NTPC's investment program is constrained by the available
financing, not by demand for NTPC-generated electricity. Power supply is
projected to constrain electricity consumption In India well beyond the year
2000 and the risk for lower than projected generation due to low demand is
margiual. A 101 reduction in demand would reduce the ERR to 14.12. In the
case of NTPC, its *demand forecast is essentially a supply forecast
(projected output of the generating stations) and will be lower than projected
only in case the supply does not materialize, i.e. in case the stations do not
generate as much as projected, e.g. due to insufficient fuel supply or lower
plant availability. That scenario was tested in the sensitivity analysis, by
reducing the PLP by 102 as discussed above.  the ERR results are sumarized in
Table 5.1.
tebl. 6.1. Th. Rslts oft the ERR and S.nstevtyt Analnis
Cm*                            ERR %
1. Base Case                        14.?
2. Efficiency down by 105           18.4
S. Coneration down by 10X           12.8
4. Dmand down by 10                 14.1
S. Comblnation of (2) and (3)       11.0
5.5       Vin&dhachal and Rihand Proiects.  These two proposed NTPC projects
are included in India's least-cost generation expansion plan, following
techno-economic generation expansion planning studies performed by CEA. This
was reconfirmed in additional project-specific appraisal studies performed by
CiA. Vindhyachal and Rihand projects are included in NTPC's core Investment
program; in fact they are NTPC's two lowest-cost projects. Both are
extensions at existing power station sites and can thus take advantage of the
existing site infrastructure. Both are also mine-mouth stations, resulting in
low coal transport costs and lowest overall fuel costs among NTPC stations.
The associated transmission development needs are relatively minor, again due
to the fact that these projects are extensions of existing power stations and
therefore already have the basic transmission facilities in place (Rihand) or
mostly in placelpartly under construction (Vindhyachal). The total cost of
Rihand and Vindhyachal power (generation and associated transmission) is the
lowest among new thermal power generation options available to the SEBs in
India. SEBs capable of implementing and operating similar 500 MK projects,
such as those of Maharashtra, Andhra Pradesh and Tamil Nadu (and other
generating companies) may achieve comparable construction costs and
operational performance, but due to higher fuel costs they will have higher
total cost of power. Due to the station-specific tariff setting, project-
specific EMRs are in line with the base-case ERR of NTPC's time-slice
investment program. The results of the time-slice sensitivity analysi
summarized in Table 5.1, are similarly applicable to these two components of
the program.
Need for Increased Power SuPply
5.6       The growth targets of GOI's economic stabilization and
liberalization program will be threatened as long as power supply continues to
constrain industrial development and massive investments in power supply
continue to be required (paras. 1.1-3). NTPC's investment program is needed



- 46 _
to help meet the growing demand and contain the power gap (difference between
projected demand and available supply) - the closing of the power gap is
unlikely in the foreseeable future, given the current low level of electricity
consumption, projected demand and available Investment resources. In the
absence of grid-supplied electricity, consumers are partly able to respond by
resorting to costlier Imported petroleum fuels (for captive diesel generation
in industries, diesel pumping in agriculture and kerosene lighting in
households and small commercial establishments), and are partly forced to
reduce the level of their economic activities due to the higher cost and/or
unavailability/unsuitability of alternative fuels. The use of NTPC's
electricity tariff as a proxy for the economic benefit of electricity
therefore underestimates the value of the incremental electricity supply to be
generated from the stations in its investment program.
5.7       Of India's proposed investments in thermal generation capacity,
those of NTPC are among the lowest-cost options and accordingly command high
priority and warrant support when overall investment resources are constrained
and an increasing share of the total is being allocated to rehabilitating the
existing generation facilities and strengthening the transmission and
distribution system (para. 1.20).
Proiect Risks
5.8       Technical Risks in Implementation and Operation.  The project does
not pose any particular technical risks as proven technologies will be
utilized and project implementation will follow practices well-established at
NTPC. NTPC'e investment program is ambitious but achievable (constrained
mainly by financing, as discussed in para. 5.9) and implomentable, in fact
NTPC has undertaken a larger program during the peak of its expansion in the
19809. Tender documents are to be finalized prior to Board presentation
(paras. 3.23) to accelerate project implementation and disbursements after
loan approval. NTPC has a proven track record in operating its power
stations, similar to those to be constructed under the project, with
satisfactory availability and efficiency. Therefore, once commissioned the
project facilities are expected to be operated without particular operational
risks.
5.9       Financial/Commercial Risks.  NTPC's investment program is
constrained by the available financing, not by the demand for electricity or
NTPC's implementation capability. The core investment program has been
prepared taking into account NTPC's capability to raise resources, to minimize
the risk of starting too many projects in an attempt to meet the projected
demand for electricity. NTPC's new two-part tariff system is designed to
enable NTPC to recover its costs, including the cost of the project, and earn
a reasonable return on its investments. The costs of the investments to be
made under the proposed project will be recovered under these standard
arrangements. The financial viability of NTPC's investment program is not
significantly affected by changes in key variables: tariff allows NTPC to
charge its customers in addition to investment costs also the cost of working
capital, actual interest charges and a GOI-prescribed return on equity.
Sensitivity analysis established the impact of adverse changes in NTPC's
critical operating parameters on the ERR of the investment program to be
acceptable (paras. 5.3-5.4). NTPC's main financial risk therefore relates to
its ability to recover its charges from its clients. As long as it recovers,
the level and structure of the tariff will safeguard its financial position



- 47 -
and provide adequate self-financing for the implementation of its Investment
program. Special attention has therefore been paid during project processing
to developing satisfactory commercial policies and arrangements to ensure
satisfactory revenue recovery (para. 4.9).
5.10      Environmental Risks.  Environmental Impact assessments of the
generation projects proposed to be financed under the Bank loan demonstrate
that plant emissions will be within COX/Bank guidelines. Special attention
has also been paid during project processing to ensuring that NTPC develops
comprehensive environmental and resettlement policies and implements these
policies by undertaking satisfactory resettlement action plans and programs
for the environmental rehabilitation of its existing power stations.
Supervision missions will review the implementation of the agreed
Eavironmental Action Plan to ensure that: (a) the environmental impacts of the
proposed project remain acceptable and within applicable GO and Bank
guidelines; and (b) the agreed mitigatory measures are strlctly pursued by
NTPC and concerned GOI agencies tor (i) the resettlement of affected persons
and (ii) the reduction of emissions from existing power stations.
5.11      Institutional/Contractual Risks.  Apart from the commercial
(revenue recovery) risk, the main project risks relate to the implementation
of NTPC's joint-ventures with private partners. Vhile the technical and
operational aspects are expected to be handled in a satisfactory manner (NTPC
even by itself being able to take of these aspects), there is a considerable
risk of delay in the implementation of the envisaged joint-sector projects due
to contractual issues. The financial impact of such delays on NTPC is likely
to be modest, as contractual delays typically delay the start of project
implementation, i.e. take place before significant amounts of funds are
actually spent and tied in assets under construction. The major impact is
therefore the resulting delay In the availability of additional electricity
supply to fuel the Indian economy (para. 5.6). The Bank is providing
assistance to GOI and executing agencies in the power sector in the
preparation of model documents for private power, as well as helping 001
address the remaining policy and regulatory issues constraining private power
development. Such assistance will help mitigate some of the contractual risks
of NTPC's joint-venture operations as well. Protracted negotiations can not,
however, be ruled out in the finalization of fuel supply and power purchase
agreements with clients such as the SEB's and DESU. In addition, contractual
issues among the joint-venture partners may also arise. Supervision missions
will review the implementation of NTPC's joint-venture projects and further
technical assistance will be provided, if necessary, to help NTPC overcome
institutional/contractual issues in its joint-sector operations.
VI. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATION
6.1       During negotiations agreement has been reached that:
(a)  The following, general eligibility criteria will apply to projects
to be financed under the proposed Bank loan(s) (para. 3.3):
(i) satisfactory technical, economic and financial viability of the
proposed power plants including fuel and transmission linkages;



- 48 -
(ii) all necessary 001 clearances Including Public Investment Board
(PlB) and environmental for the power station would have been
obtained; the Environmental Assessments vould have to be
carried out in accordance with Bank guidelines and for 'Al
projects au Environmental Assessment Summary would have to be
circulated to the Board before issuance of tender documents;
(iiI) the SEB of the state in which the plant is to be located would
be In compliance with the terms of existing BPSA;
(iv) satisfactory financing plan; and
(v)  preparation of a satisfactory Resettlement Action Plan where
required, duly endorsed by the State authorities.
(b) NTPC shall furnish to the Bank, for the Bank's review, not later
than December 31 of each year, starting December 31, 1993, its
financial projections, including its investment program and
financing plan (para. 3.3);
(c) GQI confirmed that adequate and timely supplies of fuel and
financing for the associated transmission systems for Rilund II and
Vindhyachal II will be made available and that adequate measures
have been taken to ensure the coordination of construction
schedules (para. 3.8);
(d)  for joint venture projects to be eligible for Bank financing the
same eligibility criteria as for NTPC's own projects would apply,
suitably modified with respect to Government clearances and the
signing of a Power Purchase Agreement satisfactory to the Bank
(para. 3.9);
ge) NTPC shall create, not later than December 31, 1993, an equitable
mortgagelcharge in favor of the Bank on its unencumbered assets by
way of security for the proposed loan.(para. 3.15);
(f)   MPC will implement the Enviromental Action Plan dated May 10,
1993 agreed with the Bank (para. 3.30);
(g) NTPC wi1l implement the agreed RAPs satisfactory to the Bank (para.
3.34);
(h) NTPC shall carry out socio-economic surveys not later t..an December
31, 1994, to ascertain the present socio-economic statt. of PAls of
ongoing Bank financed projects and draw up and implement an agreed
remedial action program thereafter (3.34).
Mi) NTPC shall submit to the Bank not later than December 31, 1993 the
scope of the financial management systems review to be undertaken
in framework of the twinning arrangement with BEI and discuss with
the Bank not later than June 30, 1994 the implementation of the
recoamendations (para. 4.3);



- 49 -
(j) NhPC shall furnish to the Bank, not later than seven months after
the close of each fiscal year, its audited financial statements.
together with the audit report from the private audltors and the
comments of the Comptroller & Auditor General of India, as well as
the audit reports of the special account and vithdrawals made
against statement of erpenditures for the project (para. 4.4);
(k) MMPC shall, not later than March 31, 1994. enter Into regional
BPSA& satisfactory to the Bank with all the SEBs with at least two
more such agreements be signed not later than December 31, 1993
(para 4.9);
(1) COI to settle remaining S8B8 arrears through Central appropriations
I' accordance with an agreed schedule (para. 4.10);
(a) NTPC shall take all necessary steps to maintain its accounts
receivables at a level not exceeding an amount equivalent to the
preceding two months (para. 4.10);
(n) NTPC shall discuss with the Bank the results of the study on
alternative strategies for financing NTPC's long term development
not later than September 30, 1994 and shall thereafter implement
the agreed recommendations (para. 4.19); and
(0) NMPO shall take all necessary measures to produce, starting from
FP1994, funds from internal cash generation equivalent to not les
than 15Z of its capital expenditures (three-year moving average)
and not less than 201 In each fiscal year thereafter (para 4.21).
Recommendation
6.2       On the basis of the above agreements, the proposed project would be
suitable for a Bank loan of US$400 mlIlion equivalent to NTPC with the
guarantee of India, for a period of 20 years, including five years of grace,
at the Bank's standard variable Interest rate. NWPM would bear the foreign
exchange and interest rate risks.
