ANNEX 2.4
INDIA
NTPC POWER GENERATION PROJECT
NTPCs Current Environmental Performance and R&R
Introduction
1.1       As early as 1981, an Environmental Engineering Department was
established at NTPC's corporate headquarters. It has developed over the years
into a multi-disciplinary environmental group whose principal responsibility
is the preparation of Environmental Impact Assessments (EIA) which are
required by the Ministry of Environment and Forest (MOEF) before granting the
environmental clearance for any power generation project. MOEF, Central and
State Pollution Control Boards have been imposing progressively stricter
environmental standards and requiring greater attention to rehabilitation of
people affected by the construction and operation of NTPC's power stations.
Over the years, NTPC has responded by recruiting specialized staff in social
and environmental sciences and by reviewing the environmental and
rehabilitation and resettlement (R&R) aspects in its existing power stations.
The Bank has been particularly involved in the Environmental Study of the
Singrauli Area (the EdF study) financed under the Second Singrauli Thermal
Power Project (Cr. 1027-IN) carried out by EdF International and Groupe
Charbonnage de France and completed in July 1991. An overview of GOI's
current environmental regulatory framework and pertinent COX and World Bank
environmental standards is given in Appendix 1.
1.2       NTPC realizes that its environmental management systems need to be
further developed beyond the mere compilation of environmental data for
compliance purposes towards the pro-active identification and management
increasingly complex environmental and socio-economic problems and issues.
During the preparation of the proposed project. NTPC has formulated a
comprehensive Environmental Action Plan (EAP) in consultation with the Bank,
in order to strengthen its environmental management practices and to address
outstanding environmental and R&R problems. The EAP includes mitigatory
actions for all NTPC's power stations and will be implemented as part of the
proposed project.
NTPC's Performance
1.3       When evaluating NTPC's environmental performance, it should be kept
in mind that when most NTPC's power plants were designed and built
environmental standards were less stringent.
1.4       Technical Environmental Compliance:  The data shown in tables 1
through 3 reflect the principal environmental parameters as required to be
submitted to the state pollution control boards. They include stack emissions
and ambient air quality for suspended particulate matters (SPM) and sulfur
dioxide. Wastewater quality data were compiled for Ph and total suspended
solids (TSS) at the ash pond outlets and at the main plant drains. The tables
also show that the older NTPC plants were built under more lenient standards
then currently required and generally operate within design standards.



- 9 -
Without upgrading of the existing ESP equipment, the new GOItstate emission
standards will be difficult to meet. NTPC has completed an ZSP performance
analysis in all its power stations and a program for retrofitting its power
stations with suitable ESP equipment to meet the new standards is being
iMplesented as part of the RAP. Full compliance with the new standards is
expected by the first quarter of 1995.
1.5       In the Singrauli area, there is problem with ambient air quality
with respect to dust. A study of the sources carried out by EdF (1991) has
however shown is soil erosion as the cause with ground level sources In the
coal mines contributing to one third of the total local concentration. The
impact of power plant emissions was considered to be negligible. NTPC is
currently taking measurements of ambient SPM at its Singrauli area plant sites
to identify potential occupational SPM exposure problems. This part of the
occupational health monitoring program is being undertaken at NTPC facilities
under the UAP. Remedial actions within NTPC's purview could include
improvements in the dust extraction and suppression system at the coal
handling plant and installation of sonic water spray nozzles or a *chemjet*
(additive) system.
1.6       The performance data for total suspended solids (TSS) in ash pond
effluent and in some cases main plant drain effluent are also indicative of
consistent problems at some of the NTPC plants. In Ramagundam. the pH of the
ash pond effluent was consistently above standards. A retrofit program for
conversion of the ash slurry system for recycling of the slurry water at
Ramagundam was initiated by NTPC and has now been agreed to be implemented
under the EAP for other NTPC power stations.
1.7       NTPC was monitoring environmental parameters and submitting the
results to State Pollution Boards regularly. Now, agreement has also been
reached with NTPC on an Expanded Monitoring Program. Sampling and analysis
will be performed with schedules at varying frequencies depending oan the
particular parameters to establish a proper scientific basis for analyzing the
environmental impact of NTPC's operations. The environmental parameters to be
monitored are presented in Appendix 2. Monitoring of potential occupational
health problems will be developed. The monitoring results are reported
regularly to the Environmental Management Group at corporate headquarters, to
ensure that adequate remedial actions are taken.

1.8       Resettlement and Rehabilitation.  While resettlement and
rehabilitation (R&R) associated with hydro-electric projects has long been a
prominent  environmental issue, R&R associated with thermal power plants has
in the past not received the same attention in planning as hydro power, even
In some of the earlier World Bank projects with NTPC. Despite this historical
lack of attention, considerable attention on R&R has been a part of thermal
power plant development in NTPC. Table 4 below shows that with the 12 NTPC'a
power completed or under construction a total of 26,623 families were
affected. In total, 4,966 families were displaced. Table 5 gives an overview
of the kind of community development activities, which were undertaken by NTPC
at the various projects.
1.9       Though state laws and regulations applicable at the time power
stations were built provided for payment of compensation only, NTPC on its own
implemented various R&R measures for PAFs. In recent years NTPC has also made
efforts to improve its planning and execution of R&R programs associated with
thermal power projects. Examples are: (i) under the Bank financed Talcher
Power Project a comprehensive R&R plan was formulated in close consultation
with the project affected families (PAFs) and the local authorities and this
is now being implemented; (ii) the EdF regional environmental impact study in
the Singrauli area which included an overall socio-economic survey;
(iii) detailed socio-economic studies were completed in 1992 at two plants
(Farakka and Ramagundam) to determine whether existing R&R plans could be
further improved and additional community facilities action plans are being
formulated. A similar study is being completed at Korba.
1.10      Two particularly innovative aspects to the Talcher R&R Plan ares
si) a primary, jobs-oriented, strategy based on a survey of existing education
and training among the PAFs correlated with a manpower needs study to set up a
program for hiring oustees in un-skilled and semi-skilled positions directly
with the power plant or indirectly through long-term maintenance contractors;
and (i) an experimental Land Purchasing Committee to implement a Land-for
Land option, with cash assistance from the project.
1.11      Various spontaneous and pro-active pilot programs for improving
conditions in communities surrounding NTPC's thermal plants have been
undertaken, both with respect to community facilities and to facilitate income
generating schemes, with special attention to rehabilitation colonies and to
communities in the immediate vicinity of the project. The approach at Rihand
STPP is especially noteworthy. Here the administrative task for R&R has been
divided between two groups. One group, in the Personnel and Administrative
section handles the routine matters such as land acquisition or payment of
compensation for complying with official regulations, as is done by this
section at all the plants. The second group has been formed on an informal
basis under Management Services, which normally handles such things as
planning and systems, computer data processing and field engineering.

1.12      This second group reports directly to the General Manager and works
with both PAFs and the wider project affected communityO through an NOO
(project-established and sponsored) to explore self-employment options and
broader community development possibilities. The activities of the group are
based on the premise that efficient plant operations rely on a healthy
relationship with the community, that this 'buffer- community reflects how the
rest of the views NTPC. This group very strongly emphasizes economic
upliftment of the neighboring community so as to minimize the disparity
between those deriving benefits from the project and the others.
1.13      These efforts at Rihand and Talcher thermal power plants are recent
innovations, addressing what had been seen as inadequate R&R plans. As in the
case of the former. They are by no means the only such creative innovations
at NTPC sites, but they illustrate best what NTPC staff are capable of when
they wish to improvise new solutions to R&R problems. With its more liberal
R&R policy and the proven capability of many of its staff, NTPC has obviously
been taking impressive steps forward in this area.
NTPC's Rehabilitation and Resettlement Policy
1.14      A comprehensive R&R policy was formulated by NTPC in 1991, but
although it shared the same objective, it was not fully compatible with the
Bank's guidelines on involuntary resettlement. During project preparation
agreement was reached on a revised R&R policy (Appendix 3), which is now
broadly consistent with the Bank's operational directives. This policy will
be applicable not only for Bank financed power stations, but also for other
operations of NTPC as applicable. The policy does not yet fully meet the
Bank's directives relating to (a) the treatment of tribals, particularly with
respect to the land tenure rights and (b) the rehabilitation of people
affected by the ongoing projects for which additional income restoration
measures may need to be taken.
1.15      With respect to the treatment of tribals, it is not clear to GO,
how the Bank's directives on land tenure rights could be implemented, given
the current legal framework in India, and the various and changing socio-
economic and cultural conditions under which tribals live in India. In its
site selection process, NTPC would normally avoid tribal areas and PAFs of the
Rihand II and Vindhyachal II projects do not include any tribals. However, in
case tribals (or rather, aindigenous people" as defined under the Bank's
operational directives) would be involved an appropriate Rehabilitation Action
Plans (RAPs) would be prepared by NTPC consistent with GO0 guidelines on the
basis of a detailed socio-economic baseline survey. If Bank financing would
be involved, the RAPs would have to be consistent with the Bank's directives.
1.16      With respect to PAPs from earlier NTPC projects, the new R&R policy
provides for NTPC undertaking special socio-economic surveys to ascertain the
present socio-economic status of these PAFs. On the basis of these surveys
and where found necessary , NTPC will, in consultation with these PAPs and
appropriate State authorities, formulate a remedial action plan which would
(a) focus on developing or enhancing community facilities for the benefit of
these PAPs; (b) provide training an other facilities to upgrade skills and
opportunities of these PAPs in order to Improve their standard of living and
thereby their earning capacity; and (c) using its good offices to assist PAPs
to achieve such improvements through ongoing Government assistance programs.
For the three ongoing Bank financed projects (Second Farakka, Combined Cycle



- 19 -
and National Capital Project), agreement was reached during negotiations that
these socio economic surveys would be carried out not later than December 31,
1994 and that based on these surveys, NTPC in consultation with the Bank would
draw up where necessary, remedial action programs which have as their
objective the phased restoration of the economic base of the persons so
affected. This forms part of the Environmental Action Program to be
implemented under the proposed loan.
Organizational Development
1.17      NTPC has made considerable efforts in recent years to respond to
increasing environmental requirements to address environmental problems and to
improve its R&R programs. During the supervision of ongoing UTPC projects and
the preparation of the proposed project it was observed that environmental
data, including those for monitoring R&R programs were generally not collected
systematically and that remedial actions were often taken in response to
stricter GOI/State regulations rather than on the basis of scientifically
collected environmental and socio-economic parameters.
1.18      In August 1992, NTPC constituted a Committee headed by one of the
general managers of its power stations to review the environmental management
needs. Environmental management aspects were also reviewed by BEl consultants
in November/December 1992 under an existing twinning arrangement. Their
report also confirmed that environmental management need strengthening. In
February! March 1993, the NTPC committee has made the following
reconendations:  the environmental activities of the Company fall broadly in
the three categories - Environmental Engineering that closely associates with
the power project engineering division, EMG that works along with the power
station operations groups and Community Development Group which builds up the
R&R and community development activities as a larger welfare measure. The
coordination of their activity be taken by Corporate Planning in-so-far as
funding agencies are concerned. Quarterly review be conducted by Regional EDs
who are directly responsible for all operations of the stations and for
projects implemented in the region. Recent organizational changes v±thin NTPC
to meet the future environmental challenges are given in para's 2.14 to 2.19
of the main report. Such changes once fully operational, include systematic
data collection and monitoring, development of environmental and R&R policies,
regulations and standards, as well as human resource development and training.

ANNEX 2.4
Appendis 1
ReRulatory Framework and Environmental Standards
1.1       Technicals  The Government of India's (GOI) Department of
Environment (DOEN) in the Ministry of Environment and Forests (MOEF) has
developed Environmental Guidelines for Thermal Power Plants. Standards for
emissions are stipulated by the Central or State Pollution Control Boards, and
regular monitoring reports are required to be submitted to the State Pollution
Control Boards.
1.2       An Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) is required by the DOEN
for environmental clearance of proposed projects. The DEN has specific
guidelines concerning the content of EIA submittal. The project EIA proposals
are placed before the DEN's Coumittee for Environmental Appraisal. The
Committee's actions can includes rejection of the project; relocation of the
project; or environmental clearance of the project, with or without
incorporation of specific environmental safeguards. The stipulations of the
environmental clearances can be enforced under the Water (Prevention & Control
of Pollution) Act, 1974; the Air (Prevention & Control of Pollution) Act,
1981; and the Environment (Protection) Act. 1986; or their subsequent
amendments. The clearances often carry the clauses that the stipulations can
be varied or new ones imposed, that the clearance can be revoked in the event
of non-implementation of the conditions, or that it can be revoked in the
Interest of protection of the environment.
1.3       Additional environmental regulations which are relevant to NTPC's
operations Includos
aEnvironmental Audits, conforming to GOI requirements, are to be
included in corporation annual reports (as of March 1993).
*    Every facility which stores hazardous materials over specified
threshold quantities must have Disaster Management Plans (developed
In coordination with the State Pollution Control Boards and not a
regulatory local emergency response agencies, Including hospitals)
(regulation currently in effect).
*    All industrial facilities are required to carry liability Insurance
to cover hazardous material spills and other environmental damages.
The liability cap is set at 500 million rupees. State Pollution
Control Boards are including this requirement Consent Order
renewals.
1.4
The various environmental acts include crImlnal penalties for the factory
occupler for non compliance with environmental regulations. A new Public
Liability Act requires damages compensation for accidental environmental
releases. Under the executive action can be taken to close a factory, and
under the 1986 Environmental Protection Act, the Air & Water Act, power and
water supply to a plant can be cut. Recently, NGOs have filed in the Supreme
Court for compensation of damages suffered by the public due to environmental
pollution caused by a group of Industries. This case is currently pending. A



- 21 -
private citizen can file a criminal complaint against an industry that is out
of compliance with pollution laws. The citizen must first ask the regulating
agency to control the pollution, and if the agency fails to control it within
60 days. the citizen can file a criminal complaint. The citizen need not be a
sufferer; any concerned citizen can take this action.

ANNEX 2.4
Appendix 3
INDIA
NATIONAL THERMAL POWER CORPORATION (NTPC)
REHABILITATION AND RESETTLEMENT POLICY
PREAMBLE
Most of the NTPC Projects are located in remote areas. NTPC
acquires large stretches of rural land for its projects which may displace
people. In those instances, NTPC will take measures to resettle and
rehabilitate Project Affected Persons (PAPs) with the objective that the PAP
will improve or at least regain their previous standard of living.
Thus, NTPC's effort is complementary to the Government's efforts in rural
development. As and when any national Rehabilitation and Resettlement CR1R)
guidelines/policy/Government order is finalized and adopted, NTPC will review
and modify its R&R policy as is suitable for thermal power projects. The REX
policy will be implemented in close cooperation with the concerned state
authorities and to the extent that endorsement by the State authorities is
warranted, NTPC will seek to obtain such endorsement.
This policy will be implemented within the framework of local
applicable law.
1. CHAPTER
NEED FOR R&R OF DISPLACED PERSONS
1.1       Improper resettlement and rehabilitation is the root of
discontentment and alienation of the PAPs. It may be difficult to operate a
project efficiently without the cooperation of the local people.
1.2       The land that is acquired for thermal power projects is for a
public purpose and generally the displacement is involuntary. The PAPs often
face forcible eviction and, in fact, have no choice but to face the new social
set up.
1.3       The age old rural environment is often transformed to semi-urban
onvironment in a short period of time. While there is usually a rise in the
cost of living, the scope for traditional sources of income is likely to be
reduced. In most of the cases, PAPs find it increasingly difficult to cope
with the new environmental set up.
1.4       The landed property is handed over to the holders through
generations. Land acquisition and consequent displacement disrupts the
traditional social system.
1.5       Drastic changes in the land use pattern substantially alters the
agro-based rural economy and the life style of people affected. Further, even
though the assets like cattle etc. may not be disturbed, the PAPs may have to



- 25 -
sell them off in the absence of pasture land, needing alternative means of
income.
II. CHAPTER
BASIC ISSUES INVOLVED IN FnAMING AN REPARILITATION ACTION PLAN
Regainint of Economic Base:
2.1       Acquisition of land induces a large-scale change in land use
patterns and can destroy the economic base. The Rehabilitation Action Plan
(RAP) is to be formulated so that after a reasonable transition period, the
displaced persons improve, or at least regain their previous standard of
living, earning capacity and production levels. The transition gap is to be
reduced to a minimum.
2.2       NTPC's involvement in the rehabilitation activities will continue
until such time as NTPC has taken all actions in accordance with the RAP to
enable PAPs to improve or regain their previous standard of living.
1II. CHAPTER
CATEGORIES OF PAP' AND RA ENTITLUIENTS
3.1       For resettlement and rehabilitation purposes, it is necessary that
the PAPs be classified into different categories.
Prolect Affected Persons (PAPs) cover the following:
PAPa
(a) A person whose entire land is acquired.
(b) A person owning land which is not economically viable after
acquisition. In some states the minimum amount of land considered
economically viable is 2 Ha; adjustment needs to be made depending
on local conditions such soil fertility, access to water
(irrigation) and other factors.
(c) A person whose homestead only is acquired.
(d) A person whose homestead and land is acquired.
(e) A person (tenant tiller) who is in actual possession of land which
is acquired.
(f) A person (agricultural laborer) who does not have legal title to
the land which is acquired but earns his living principally through
manual labor on that land.



- 26 -
(g) A tribal residing or deriving livelihood from the forest land
which is acquired.
(h) A landless person cultivating on the government land which is
acquired.
(i) Persons dependent upon the land either directly or indirectly, and
having a client relationship with the displaced community.
(j) In a joint family or joint holding, a person (major) who has a
share in the land or homestead.
(k) A permanent resident of the area which is acquired but not defined
above.
(1) A person (absentee land owner or absentee homestead owner) who is
not in actual possession of land or homestead for 5 years prior to
date of notification under LA Act.

the form of (a) written legal document or (b) reference to a record, such as a
revenue officer certificate, electoral roll or ration card; in the absence of
which (c) an oral declaration, credible andlor corroborated, will be
considered.

Content of the RAP and Eligibility Criteria:
3.4       The rehabilitation may differ from project to project. The RAP will
covers
(a) legal instruments and regulations;
(b) objectives;
(c) maps;
(d) baseline socio-economic survey;
1e) community participation and integration with host populations;
(f) mechanisms to select alternate sites, evaluate losses and allocate
compensation;
(g) plan for resettlement and rehabilitations
Ch) estimated cost;
(i) financial plan;
(j) responsibility, organization, and staffing
(k) timetable for iplementation of RAP; and
(1) monitoring and evaluation arrangement.



- 28 -
3.5       Consultation with representatives of PAPs like Panchayat or State
Government/District Development Authorities and voluntary organization like
Chetna of Rihand will be made. Any entitled person if deprived of their
benefits as applicable to him under this policy, has a right to legal
recourse.
Rehabilitation Measures:
3.6       Rehabilitation of PAPs involves two distinct aspects:
(i) compensation for losses in terms of land, cash and other forms; and
(ii) assistance to start a new life in terms of opportunities, training,
credit and community services for schooling and health and new employment
opportunities. Measures to be undertaken by NTPC are set out below.
HOMESTEAD
3.7       The eligible PAP will be given compensation for his existing
homestead (structure and appurtenant land) on the basis of evaluation done
under the Land Acquisition Act. In addition, NTPC will provide to said PAP a
developed plot (50' x 40') free of cost at the new housing site, transport
arrangement for his belongings and reusable material from his old house, and
such other assistance as may be required for the rehabilitation.
LAD FOR LAND
3.8       This appears to be one of the most preferred modes of
rehabilitation. However, the main problem is the availability of land. The
following modalities will be exercised for this purpose for the eligible
oustees.
Mi) An PAP who chooses this option, will not be entitled to any
other rehabilitation option. However, necessary transport
assistance will be provided to the PAPs for moving Into the new
area;
(Ui) NTPC will negotiate with the State Government for release of
revenue land for allotment to the eligible PAPs. NTPC will
provide infrastructural facilities to make the land fit for
agriculture, if the land allotted is of lower quality;
(iiL) If Government land is not available, private land may be
acquired through a Land Purchase Committee (LPC) on a 'willing
buyer - willing seller* basis. MTPC will use its good offices
to facilitate establishing this. The LPC will consist of the
District Collector or his nominee; representative(s) from NTPC;
representative(s) from PAP; and such other persons as the State
Government consider necessary;
(iv) Under thls concept (item iii), the maximum amount of land
allotment will be limited to 2 Ha of suitable land. 1o
compensation for the land acquired would be paid to the PAPs to
the extent of land allotment.
tv) Land to be allotted (agricultural/lirrigable) will be identified
by the Land Purchase Committee in the vicinity of the project



- 29 -
area (within 25 Kms. radius). A PAP opting for this option
will get a maximum of two options for selection of alternate
land, subject to availability.
3.9       When alternate land is not available nor-acceptable to the eligible
PAP as per the above procedure or in cases where a PAP is not entitled to the
'land for land' option, the PAP may exercise one of the following options for
his rehabilitation. A variety of Income generating schemes will be offered on
the basis of (a) consultation with PAPs and local Government; and (b) the
socio-economic survey establishing the need for such schemes. The following
are illustrative:
SELF EMPLOYMENT, DAIRY, POULTRY, HANDICRAFTS, ETC.
3.10      Dairy and poultry is one of the most viable rehabilitation options.
NTPC will provide about Rs.15,000 - (subject to regular review and adjustment)
per family for this purpose. Such a project offers a good market for dairy
and poultry products & this option is expected to prove beneficial. Technical
assistance in start up as well as marketing/facilities will be offered.
3.11      In some areas, people earn their living through handicrafts or
other income generating schemes. To the displaced families, NTPC will provide
about Rs.15,000 (subject to regular review and adjustment) per family for
providing means of   income through handicrafts.  This will also help in
preserving and grooming the national heritage.
3.12      The expenses identified for establishment of self employment
schemes like dairy, poultry, handicrafts etc. will not be directly disbursed
to the PAPs but channelized through banks.
SHOPS
3.13      Shops are one of the viable rehabilitation options.  A limited
number of shops will be earmarked for allotment to the PAPs after appropriate
consultation regarding the PAP's capability and aptitude. Shops would be
constructed by NTPC and allotted to the willing PAPs. The capital required
for operation of the shops, not exceeding Rs. 15,000/- (subject to regular
review and adjustment) would be made available by banks as loans and NTPC will
stand surety for the loans. The interest payable will be borne by NTPC. Any
assistance needed by PAPs in formulation of schemes for procuring loans from
banks and stabilizing the same will be rendered by NTPC.
AWARD OD PETTY CONTRACTS
3.14      PAPs will also be considered for awards of small petty contracts on
a preferential basis.
JOBS
3.15      Jobs will be given to some eligible PAPs on preferential basis.
(a) Jobs with NTPC: NTPC projects do not envisage significant job
opportunities to the local residents. However, some jobs will be
earmarked for the PAPs in the un-skilled and semi-skilled category.
However, preference will be given to eligible PAPs if they meet the



- 30 -
job requirements in the skilled categories.
(b) Jobs with Contractorss Contractors will be persuaded to give jobs
to eligible PAPs on a preferential basis where feasible.
TRAINING
3.16      Some members of the displaced families may qualify to go for
vocational training courses. NTPC will pay the training cost including
tuition, residential hostel charges, books and stationery requirement charges.
Whereas no job commitment could be given to the trained PAPs, they could be
given preference provided they meet NTPC's job requirements. This option will
be determined after appropriate consultation regardlng the PAPs capability and
aptitude.
COMMUNITY FACILITIES
3.17      The commmnity facilities and services would be available to all
residents of the area, including PAPs and the host population. The homestead
PAPs will be given developed house sites (50'x 40') for residential purposes,
free of cost. The resettlement village(s) will be provided the following
facilities at NTPC ezpenses
(a) PRIMARY SCHOOLs All capital expenditure for school building,
furniture, blackboards, other equipments including that of a
laboratory would be provided by NTPC. However, the responsibility
of running schools would be that of the State Government. In case,
the State Government efforts do not materialize, the schools can be
operated with the help of welfare agencies or any other suitable
alternative.
(b) DISPENSARYt In addition to all capital expenditure for building,
furniture, equipments etc., the services of doctor/doctors and the
medicines for running the dispensaries will be provided by NTPC.
{c) One village pond; Panchayat ghar; one drinking water well for at
least every 50 families/renovation of existing wells/handpump water
supply for drinking; Water Bound Macadam (WBM) with bituminous
surfacing road linked to the main road; drainage; road inside the
village with trees planted on either side; Sulabh SauchalayaO for
each dwelling area & road electrification of the resettlement area
which is in the vicinity of the project.
3.18      The approach for operation of community facilities   would be
flexible and all efforts will be made to involve the State and local self
Government/Panchayat for operating the facilities. To achieve this, NTPC will
pursue with these agencies to ensure the same.



- 31 -
IV. CdAPTEI
IMPLEMENTATION, MONITORING AND EVALUATION, DISPUTE MECHANISM
IMPLEMENTATION PROCEDUREt
4.1       To develop an appropriate and effective RAP, the following
procedure will be adopted.
DETAILED DEMOGRAPHIC AND SOCIO-ECONOMIC SURVEY OF THE AREA PROPOSED TO BE
ACQUIRED:
(a) A study will be carried out at all the new project sites through a
reputed consultant well-experienced in this kind of work. It is
desirable to appoint a local consultant who is well versed with the
social matrix of the area. The District Collector will be involved
at every stage of the study and the data will be authenticated by
the Collector. PAPs will be consulted.
(b) The basic objective of the socio-economic study will be to generate
baseline data on the social and economic status of the population
who are likely to lose their means of livelihood or homestead due
to the acquisition of the land for the project. The data base will
be used to formulate a viable and practical RAP for the affected
families. For this purpose, the choice of rehabilitation options of
the affected families. in order of priority will be recorded in the
socio- economic census after due consultations.
SCHEDULE OF THE CENSUS AND FORMMLATION OF R&R PLAN
4.2       The socio-economic study is to be conducted immediately after the
preparation of the Feasibility Report (FR). Once the demographic and socio-
economic data is available, the R&R Plan will be formulated in consultation
with the State Government and PAPs. The R&R Plan will be formulated as a
simultaneous activity with the land acquisition process.
MONITORING AND EVALUATION
4.3       The R&R scheme will be monitored and evaluated periodically after
the completion of the land acquisition process.
(a) The R&R activities are the responsibility of Personnel &
Administration Department. A separate group. both at the projects
and the Corporate Centre has been constituted for implementation.
monitoring and evaluation of the RAP with respect to time and cost
frame. At Corporate Centre the group is headed by a Senior
Manager, whereas at the project, an executive of the rank of a
Manager is heading the group.
(b) The site group will be in close interaction with the state
authorities during the implementation of the plan. Although NTPC
will develop the plots and infrastructural facilities in the
resettlement colony and actively  implement the R&R plan, assistance
of State Authorities will be taken for administrative services like



- 32 -
allotment of plots, procurement of material for self employment
etc. Constant dialogue and regular meetings with the concerned
State Authorities will be held. Implementation will be planned,
monitored and corrective measures, if required, will be
incorporated in the plan. Apart from the State Government, the
PAPs, the village leaders including the Pradhans will also be
consulted and associated during implementation of the plan.
(c) The R&R group in the Corporate Center will evaluate the
implementation of R&R plan on its completion.
DISPUTE NECHMIIS4
4.4       A Committee comprising of nominee(s) of the State Government and
nominee(s) of the project General Manager will be constituted to review the
grievances of PAPs and propose mid-stream alterations in the RAP.
V. CHAPTER
EARLIR MMC  PROJCTS
5.1       One of the Corporate Objectives of NTPC is to function as a
responsible Corporate Citizen and discharge social responsibility in respect
of environmental protection and rehabilitation. Even earlier, NTPC has been
making a conscious effort to rehabilitate the oustees by providing them with
regular employment with NTPC, jobs with contractors, allotment of shops, award
of petty contracts, etc. in a limited measure, apart from physically
resettling the homestead oustees by allotting them developed plots of land for
making their house. However, NTPC is strengthening the Community Development
facilities to benefit the people living around its project areas.
5.2       With respect to PAPs from earlier NTPC projects, MTPC will
undertake special socio-economic surveys of said PAPs to ascertain their
present socio-economic status. On the basis of these surveys and where found
necessary, NTPC will, in consultation with said PAPs and appropriate State
Authorities, formulate a remedial action plan which would (a) focus on
developing or enhancing comunuity facilities for the benefit of said PAP.;
(b) to provide training and other facilities to upgrade skills and Improve
opportunities of said PAPs in order to Improve their standard of living and
thereby their earning capacity; and (c) using its good offices to assist PAPs
to achieve such improvements through ongoing Government assistance programs
such as the Integrated Rural Development Program. In the
locationsirehabilitation colonies where the conglomeration of PAPs exist, the
following commmnity facilities would be provided.
a.   Primary school
b.   Dispensary
c.   Drinking water well
d.   WBM link road connection to the main road
e.   Village roads
f.   Area and road lighting



- 33 -
ADOPTION OF VILLAGZS
5.3       One of the villages in the vicinity of the project araa vill be
adopted to develop them to model villages. NTPC in this instance wiii provide
coununity facilities so that villagers can benefit from the developmental
activities. However, our emphasis would be on the R&R of oustees. The
option for adoption of villages will be kept open depending upon the
circumstances.

NTPC POWER GENERATION PROJECT
Bulk Power and Transmission Tariff and
Transmission Regulation Studies
to be Implemented under the
POWERGRID System Development Project
TERMS OF REFERENCE
Alternative Tariff Structures for Power Transmission and
Bulk Power Transactions
Introduction
1.1       The purpose of Part A of the studies is to examine the
alternative ways of charging for power obtained b- SEBs for power that they
obtain from Central Sector agencies and from eaca other under a regime
which encourages transactions between entities for optimum economy and
service reliability. A working group generally referred to as the K. P.
Rao Committee* has recommended the use of a two-part tariff structure
(separate charges for fixed and variable costs) to replace the present one-
part tariffs which are fundamentally only a method for allocating the cost
of producing the output of a plant in proportion to the energy received by
each party, regardless of the capacity used, the time the energy was
produced, or the relation between the amount of capacity allocated to the
users and the amount of electricity received by them at various times. The
Committee's report left a number of issues to be decided by the Regional
Electricity Boards, including the basis of allocation of the fixed costs.
Their practices do not necessarily represent the generally accepted
principle of allocating fixed costs according to capacity, and variable
charges according to energy supplied. As a result, generation dispatch does
not follow economic merit order.
(a) Some of the inefficiencies that have been experienced in the past
have been failures on the part of various agencies to follow
operating plans, i.e. to reduce generation during low-load
periods, and drawing more than their allocated share of Central
Sector generation during peak periods. Some of these problems
may be at least partly due to perverse incentives in which the
agencies, or their personnel individually, benefit from actions
which are not in the interest of the reliability and/or economy
of the overall system.
(b) The emergence of POWERGRID as an independent unit requires a new
and thorough consideration of how the cost of the transmission
system can be allocated to its users in a manner that is both
just and perceived as just, and that will produce incentives for
all parties to work for the common good in both their investments
and their operations.



- 35 -                           NE 25
Scope of Work                     -35-
Page 2 of 7
Task A-is Survey of practices in other systems
1.2       This task will summarize the theory and practice of setting
tariffs, and their success in achieving the goals of optimal electric
system investment and operation. The consultants will review and discuss
bulk power tariff concepts used in various parts of the world, including
both inter-utilities trading in bulk power and transmission services,
Including but not exclusivelys
(a) procedures for interchange of capacity and energy;
(b) firm transactions and economy transactions;
(c) short-term and long-term transactions;
(d) system capacity and unit capacity transactions;
Ce) transmission servicess rates and priority considerations;
(f) emergency capacity and energy;
(g) methods for sharing benefits from exchanges of capacity and
energy; and
(h) contractual requirements.
1.3      The consultants will define the goals of various forms of bulk-
power tariffs in terms of:
(a)  financial goals and requiretents;
Cb) optimization of system operation both for the near term and the
long run;
(c) optimization of investment in the long term; and
td) promotion ok efficiency by consumers (SEBs and their final retail
customers).
1.4      The consultants will also:
(a) discuss how, In theory and in practice as evidenced by actual
perfoxmance, various tariff structures produce incentives or
disincentives for the parties to contribute to overall system
reliability and economy; and
(b) examine the use of incentives to personnel and to organizations
in fostering good and efficient operation of power plants in
accordance with overall system objectives and requirements.
Recommend measures to be encouraged in the operation of SEBs and
Central Sector agencies in India.
{c) The main deliverables ares ti) survey of worldwide practices in
setting tariffs for bulk-power transactions covering capacity



- 36 -
ANMEX 2.5
Page 3 of 7
and energy; (ii) analysis of the benegits to be achieved by the
use of appropriate tariff structures; (iii) analysis of the
relationships between various tariff structures and achieving the
desired goals; and (iv) examination of incentives for efficient
plant operation and maintenance consistent with system overall
objectives.
Task A-2: Tariff structure for bulk-power transactions.
1.5       This task will review present bulk power tariff structures used
in India, their effect in terms of the goals defined above for both central
thermal and hydro generation and recommend improvements on the basis of
identified inefficiencies and results of task A-1 on pricing theory and
practices and experiences elsewhere. The consultants will:
(a)  evaluate proposals for power trading tariff modifications
presented by various parties in India, including frequency-based
pricing schemes for inter-States inadvertent powerlenergy
transfers (considering the various possible forms of power
transfers: capacity, firm energy, opportunity transfers,
emergency transfers, etc.);
(b)  examine the applicability of other forms of tariffs to Indian
conditions and requirements;
(c)  recommend tariff structures to satisfy both near-term and long-
run requirements. In doing so, consider particularly the need
for adjustments to rates for such variables as fuel costs,
foreign exchange rates and inclusion of incentives for more
efficient and economic operation; and
(d)  develop a plan for the implementation of the recommended tariff
structures: sequential steps and conditions required to make the
Implementation of each step practical and effective.
(e)  The main deliverables ares (i) description of tariff structures
presently in use in India, and discussion of their effect on
power system service reliability and economic operation and
investment decisions; (ii) evaluation of proposed alternate
tariff structures for bulk-power transactions; (iii)
recommendation of tariff structures for bulk power transactions;
and (iv) recommended plan for the methodical implementation of
proposed new tariff structures.
Task A-3s Examine and recommend transmission tariff structures
1.6       this task vill review present power transmission tariff
structures used in India, their effect in terms of the goals defined above
for both central thermal and hydro generation and recommend improvements on
the basis of identified inefficiencies and results of task A-1 on pricing
theory and practices and experiences elsewhere. The consultants will
examines



-37 -                          ANNMEX 2.5
Page 4 of 7
(a) alternatives for pricing transmission services, includiAg but not
limited toa (i) cost-based allocation: (ii) fixed charges for
transmitting capacity andlor energy: 4ad (iII) value-based
pricing:
Cb)  examine the effect of various proposed power transmission tariff
concepts on grid discipline, overall system operating economy,
and system service reliability. Consider aspects of tariffs that
will encourage good performance on the part of generation owners.
operators of the transmission facilities, and operators of Load
Dispatch Centerss
Sc) consider the different effects of tariffs during the present
period of generation capacity shortages, and long-run periods In
which generation capacity may be adequate; and
Cd) develop a plan for the Implementation of the recommended tariff
structures: sequential steps, conditions required to make the
Implementation of each step practical and effective.
te) The deliverables ares (i) description of possible transmission
tariff structures for use in India, and discussion of their
advantages and disadvantages; (Li) evaluation of proposed
alternate transmission tariff structures; (iii) recommendation of
transmission tariff structures: and (iv) recommended plan for the
methodical implementation of proposed new transmission tariff
structures.
(f) qualifications of Consultants ares (i) experience with power
transmission tariff analysis and development in mixed-source
electric systems; Cii) experience In tariff structures for
thermal plants and for hydro plants; and Ciii) international
experience in inter-utility powerlenergy transfer agreements.
(g) Total estimated effort for Part A is 9 man-months.
Assess Regulatory Requirements and Recommend ley Regulations
Introduction
1.7       In the absence of full and free competition. some form of
government regulation is generally considered necessary in order to prevent
any of the participants of the electric sector from taking advantage of lts
monopoly or semi-monopoly situation by charging excessive rates or
providing inadequate service. The extent and Intensity of regulation
varies In different jurisdictions around the world, but it generally covers
at least such subjects as defining the rate-making process, including
approval of the resulting rates, priorities in the use of limited
resources, and the approval of service reliability standards.
1.8       One of the most difficult problems to resolve when various
generating entities depend upon the use of the same transmission system to
bring their output to their customers is the setting of priorities when the
transmission system is not capable of safely transmitting each generator's



- 38 -
ANNEX 2.5
Page 5 of 7
power to Its preferred customer.
1.9       the purpose of Part B of the studies Is to assess regulatory
requiremnats applicable to POIERGRID, and to recommend key regulations for
POVERGURD's operations, including conditions on access to POIERGRID's
transmission system. It is not practical, however, to consider this
subject without at the same time considering the role of the CEA and other
bodies Involved in regulating the transmission and power trading activities
of central utilities, the State Electricity Boards and private generators.
Scope of work
Task B-ls Survey of practices in other systems
(a) This task will summarize the theory and practice of setting
regulation, and their success in achieving the goals of optimal
electric system Investments and operation. The consultants will
review and discuss regulatory concepts used In various parts of
the world, covering both inter-utilities trading in bulk power
and transmission services.
Cb) The consultants will define the goals of various forms of
regulations in terms of:
C) financial goals and requirements;
Cii) optimization of system operation both for the near term and
the long run;
Ciii) optimization of investment in the long term; and
(iv) promotion of efficiency by consumers (SEBs and their final
retail customers).
(c) The consultants will also:
(1) discuss how, In theory and in practice as evidenced by
actual performance, various regulatory structures produce
Incentives or disincentives for the parties to contribute to
overall system reliability and economy; and
11) examine and recommend measures to be encouraged In the
operation of SEBs and Central Sector agencies In India.
Cd)  The main deliverables ares (i) survey of worldwide practices in
regulating bulk power transactions covering capacity and energy
and power transmission; (Ci) and analysis of the benefits to be
achieved by the use of various regulatory structures.
Task B-2* Review Current Situation and Assess Regulatory
Requirements



- 39 -
ANNEX 2.5
Page 6 of 7
1.10     This task will:
(a) define the subjects of regulation required to ensure that
POVEZGURD's monopoly will not lead to excessive rates, Inadequate
service reliability, or inefficient operation;
(b) determine to what extent the activities of other members of the
power sector-SgBs, Central Sector generation agencies, private
power systems and private generators-should be subject to
regulation. It should be noted In this respect the SEBs in
particular will retain some transmission facilities which may
affect, or be affected by, the conditions on POWERGIZID
transmission systems;
(c) discuss the regulation requirements and capabilities of carrying
these out with appropriate government agencies, POVERGRID staff,
and various SEBs, Central Sector agencies and private generators.
(d) determine what agencies are best suited to perform these
regulatory duties, and recommend the methods by which this
regulation should be carried out.
'e) Matters that should be considered as possible subjects for
regulation includes
(i)  rates for power transmission;
(ii) rates for power trading among SEBs and other agencies and
generators;
(iii)  rights, and priorities, of access to the various
transmission systems for various types of generators and
users of electricity;
(iv) service reliability standards, and methods of enforcement of
these standards;
(v)  determination of the responsibility for paying for
transmission losses; methods for determining the total
amounts of transmission losses associated with specific
transactions, and methods of pricing these losses;
(vi) methods of enforcing decisoas mad. by RIBEs and RLDCs; and
Wvii)  methods of enforcing the authority of regional system
control center operators with regard to operation of the
system during emergencies.
tf) The main deliverables ares (i) scope of regulation to be applied
to POWERGRID and to other agencies of the power sector; (ii)
recaomendations on the agencylagencies to be responsible for
regulation of POWEIGRID's activities, and those of other
agencies; (iii) recommendations on methods for -arrying out
regulation of POWERGRID; (iv) methods of enforcing operations



-40-
* ~ ~ ~ ~ ~ ~ ~~4.                                       ANNEX 2.5
Rage 7 of 7
disciplinet and (v) recommendations for the treatment of
transmission losses.
Task B-3: Outline Proposed Key Repgulations
1.11      This task will recommend key regulations governing the operation
of POWERGRID and defining the authority, rights and duties of all parties
connected to the transmission system. These regulations should be so
designed as to support the relationships between the vartious agencies as
described In the Institutional Development of POVERGRID.
1.12      Examples of subjects for key regulations to be recomended would
Include:
(a) methods of determining the capability of the trr.nsaiesion system
to perform various transmission services requested by central
utilities, SEns and private generators;
(b)  types of contracts for the provision of transmission services to
SEBes and private generators;
(c) priorities in the use of transmission system by various agencies,
generators, and customers; and
gd) methods for arriving at agreements concerning the operation of
the POWERGRID and other agencies transmission systems, such as
those of the SBs, in regard to mutual concerns such as the
maintenance of adequate voltages and the limitation of the flow
of reactir'. power at Interfaces between the systems.
1.13      For each form of regulation, the consultants will explain the
need for the regulation of this subject, and propose an appropriate
approach for formulation an effective and implementable regulation.
(a) Deliverables ares Ci) list of proposed subjects for regulation,
including an explanation of the need for the regulation; and (ii)
outlines of the proposed regulations.
(b) The consultant cualifications require knowledge of regulation
theory and practice as found in the United States and in various
European countries.
(c) Total estimated effort for Part B is 9 man-months, the total for
parts A & B is 18 man-months.

NTPC POWE GENERATION PROJECT
NATIONAL THERMAL POWER CORPORATION
Proiect Description
Exoansion of the Rlihand Thenml Power Plant (Stage Ili 2x500 MV)
1.1       The Rihand development is located in the State of Uttar Pradesh on
the southern bank of the Gobind Ballabh Pant Sagar (GBPS) Reservoir on the
River Rihand. The final profile of the Rihand Thermal Power Plant (TPP)
totalling 3,000 MW would include six units of 500 MW each to be built In three
stages. Stage I (2x500 MW) which was constructed under financIng from the
Government of the United Ringdom has been in operation since January 1991 and
is working satisfactorily. Stage II is part of the propose4 project and would
comprise two units of 500 KW each with associated mechanical, electrical. coal
transportation, coal handling, ash handling, and environmental impact
mitigating facilities. Stage III (2x500 MW) will be built at a later date.
1.2       An area of approximately 1,563 hectares where the power plant would
be built is in possession of NTPC. In addition, 251 hectares would be
acquired by NTPC for the disposal of ash from the proposed Stage II. The site
is accessible by road from Renukut which is at a distance of 70 km. The
railway siding for the Rihand TPP Was built during Stage I from the Singrauli
railway station via the Vindhyachal TPP. About 2.25 km length of rail track
would be constructed within the plant area for Stage II.
1.3       Coal Supply.  Coal for Stage II would initially be supplied from
the Dudhichua mining blgck of the Singrauli coalfields located about 40 km
from the power stationl'.  Consumption for the proposed Stage II at 5,500
11 Singrauli coalfields lie between latitudes 23° 47' to 24 12' and
longitudes 81° 57' to 82° 57' and are spread over the districts Mirzapur in
Uttar Pradesh and Sidhi in Madhya Pradesh. These coalfields cover an area of
more than 2,200 hi2; the deposits are estimated to contain about 7,500 million
tonnes of coal, of which about 2,700 million tonnes are quarriable and could
be available for exploitation. The total thickness of the exploitable
reserves in the major sectors of the coalfield varies from 24 m to about 46 m,
although in the Jhingurda sector. the thickness of the seam is about 180 m
which is one of the thickest in the world. The thickness of the seam and
working conditions in the Singrauli coalfields are such that major open cast
mines can be opened with minimum gestation period. Production from such mines
can generally be started in a period of about four years from the commenceent
of the mining operations and the full rated capacity of production can be
reached in a total period between six to eight years. The coals from this
coalfield are ideally suitable for power generation.   The coalfields have
been divided into various blocks/projects for exploitation: Gorbi.
Jhingurdah, Rakri, Bina, Marrak, Khadia, Dudhichua, Jayant, Nigahi, Amolori
and Moher. The first phase of Dudhichua was developed under Bank financing
(Ln. 2393-IN).



- 50 -
ANNEX 3.1
Page 2 of 8
hrs of operation (plant load factor of 63Z) would be about 3.30 milliou tomes
per annum. The average gross calorific value of coal from the Dudhichua block
is 4,000 Kcal/kg; at initial supply stage, average moisture content would be
about 132 and ash content 332. At some time in the future, coal would be
supplied from the Stage II expansion of the Amolori block.
1.4       Transportation of Coal. A single track Merry-Go-Round (WG') system
of rail transport built during Stage I would be doubled to meet the coal
requirements of Stage II. The MGR systems of Singaruli, Rihand and
Vindhyachal power plants would be interconnected in a manner which would a.. w
transportation of coal from any of the linked mines to any of the power
stations. Coal would be transported with unit train rakes formed of
pneumatically operated bottom-discharge type wagons. The wagons vould be
automatically loaded from fast loading sii.o at loading station at the mine and
unloaded into an underground track hopper at the power plant end. Both the
loading and unloading operations would be accomplished while the rake moves at
fixed creep speeds.
1.5       Coal Handling Facilities. A new coal handling plant (CNP) similar
to the one provided for Stage I would be built. This plant would consist of
two parallel conveying streams (one working and one standby) with rated
capacity of 1,400 tonnes/hour each for a daily operation of about 12 to 14
hours. Coal would be unloaded from wagons to an underground roller compacted
concrete (RCC) track hopper; then it would be channelled to a crusher house.
Conveyors would be provided for the following routess (a) from the track
hopper to the crusher and then to boiler bunkers; (b) from the crusher house
to the main coal stockyard through a distribution house; and (c) from reclaim
hoppers to the distribution house. Junction houses would be provided at every
change of direction of conveyors. The stackeri?eclaimer vould move on rails
supported over RCC longitudinal girders. An underground RCC reclaim hopper
would also be built. All underground structures would have adequate water
proofing treatment.
1.6       Fuel Oil and LDO Supply and Handling Facilities.  Low sulphur heavy
stock (LSHS) fuel oill/FO would be used in conjunction with pulverised coal
firing during start-up and low loads to warm up and flame stabilisation. The
facilities provided for Stage I are adequate to meet the additional
requirements of Stage II. Provision for LDO firing in one elevation of oil
burner shall also be made to facilitate start up o, the units in the event of
a complete station trip out when auxiliary steam may not be available.
1.7       Cooling Vater (CW) Supply. A closed-cycle cooling system with
cooling towers na d associated pump house would be installed for Stage II.
The cooling water circulation rate for Stage II would be 120,000 m'lh. The
makeup water requirements would be met from the GJPS reservoir through the
intake channel built for Stage I. A chlorination plant would be installed to
inject chlorine into the cooling water system so as to check the growth of
algae and other biological organisms.
1,8       Cooling Towers.  The hot water returning from the condenser would
be carried out to the base of the cooling towers by steel lined concrete
ducts, from where steel pipes would take hot water to the top of the towers.



- 51 -
ANNEX 3.1
Page 3 of 8
Four induced draft cooling towers (two for each unit) of 30,000 milnh capacity
each, would be built. Coaled water discharged from the towers would be let to
the open cold water channel.
1.9       Water Treatment Plant.  A water treatment plant would be built to
moet the additional requirements of clarified water, demineralized water (Mt)
and potable water for plant and residential colony. The plant ard the colony
would be provided with 300 ma/hr of potable water.
1.10      Steam Generators.  The two steam generators of 500 MK  each would be
drum type, subcritical, pulverized coal-fired, balanced draft type and would
use a direct firing system. Continuous evaporation rating of each steam
generator would be 1,700 t/hr; pressure at super heater outlet, 179 kglcm2;
and temperature of superheated and reheat steam, 5400 C. Each steam generator
would be able to take full load (100Z maximum continuous rating - MCR) when
burning worst quality coal. Coal burners would be so designed that a high
combustion efficiency is achieved and the use of LSHS and high speed diesel
oil is minimized.
1.11      Electrostatic Precipitators (ESPs). Each steam generator would be
fitted with an ESP of 99.5Z efficiency and having four streams, with one
stream out for maintenance and under worst coal conditions. Outlet emissions
would not exceed 100 mgIN10 while steam generator is operating at its MCR and
burning worst coal havlng maximum ash content. The residual particulate
matter in the flue gas at the ZSP outlet would be dispersed in the atmosphere
through a chimney. Space provision would be kept in the layout for addition
of Flue Gas Desulphurization (FGD) plant in the future, if required.
1.12      Chlmney. The emission of the fly ash would be through a twin flue
chlmney of 275 m high common to the two units.
1.13      Turbines and Generators. The two turbines of 500 MW each would be
of tandem compound single reheat, regenerative, condensing, multi-cylinder
design with separate high pressure (BP) and intermediate pressure (IP)
casings. It would be directly coupled to a generator suitable for indoor
installation. The turbine would be suitable for variable pressure operation.
Each steam turbine would have the following steam parameterss BP pressure: 170
kglcm 2; RP temperatures 5370 C; and Condenser vacuums 63 mm Hg. The
condensers would be designed for a coadenser cooling water temperature rise of
8 to 100 C. The two generators, each rated at 588 MVA, would be water and
hydrogen cooled. A brushless excitation system would be used.
1.14      Ash Handling System and Ash Disposal Area. Bottom ash would be
e=tracted either by using a continuously operating submergence scraper chain
conveyor system or by using intermittently operating jet pumps in conjunction
with a water impounded hopper. The bottom ash from bottom ash hopper would be
transported in slurry form to ash slurry pump in ash slurry pump house. The
fly ash from electrostatic hoppers would be extracted and conveyed to
buffer/surge hoppers located adjacent to the ESP by means of pneumatic
conveying system (either vacuum or pressure).  The fly ash from buffer/surge
hopper would be conveyed by pressure pneumatic conveying system to the fly ash

Page 4 of 8
storage silos for ash utilization purposes. In case of non-utilization of fly
ash, the fly ash from buffer surge hoppers would be slurrified and led to ash
slurry sump in ash slurry puwp house for further dlsposal. Then the total ash
would be disposed of as a slurry through pipelines to the ash disposal area
which would be constructed at approximately 3 km from the power station.
After sedimentation, ash pond slurry water would be recycled. Slowdown form
the ash pond slurry water recycling system would be treated prior to discharge
into the GBPS reservoir. The discharged water would have a suspended solid
concentration of less than 100 mgll.
1.15      Data Acauisition System. Each unit would have an independent data
acquisition system which would be based on state-of-the-art digital computer
configuration. An uninterruptible power supply (UPS) systom would be provided
for meeting the power requirements of each unit system.
1.16      Residential Colony.  The residential colony would be located nezt
to the existing one and would be close to the power plant. 759 additional
dwelling units would be constructed for Stage II.
1.17      Power Evacuation System. Riband TPP is situated in the Northern
Electricity Region, where a combination of 220 kV and 400 kV transmission
lines and substations transmit power to the large industrial consuers and
cities. The Rihand-Delhi (at Dadri) high voltage direct current (H8DC)
transmission system is part of the Northern Region and was built to transfer
In bulk power generated at the Singrauli, Rihand and Vindhyachal power
stations to the western parts of the region. The region is connected with the
Western Electricity Region via an BVDC back-to-back system built in
Vindhyachal. The transmission network around Rihand TPP would be reinforced
to evacuate the power generated at Stage II. The following transmission

1.22      Coal Handling Facilities. A new coal handling plant (CUP) similar

concrete (RCC) track hopper; then it would be channelled to a crusher house.
Conveyors would be provided for the following routest (a) from the track
hopper to the crusher and then to boiler bunkers; (b) from the crusher house
to the main coal stockyard through a distribution house; and (c) fhon reclaim
hoppers to the distribution house. Junction houses would be provided at every

would also be built.. All underground structures would have adequate water

1.24      Cooling Water (CW) Supply. As in Stage I, a closing cycle cooling

The make up water required (60 m8/sec) would be drawn from the open discharge

pumps would be installed. Out of these, four would be for regular operation
and the fifth for common stand by. The latter would be equipped with suitable

would be built. Cooled water discharged from the towers would be let to the
open cold water channel, which in turn will convey the water to the forebay of
CV pump house.
1.26      Water Treatment Plant. A water treatment plant would be built to
meet the additional requirements of clarified water and demineralized water
(DM). The potable water requirement for the plant and the colony would be met
from the filter house of Stage I.
1.27      Steam Generators.  The two steam generators of 500 MV each would be
sub-critical, drum type, single reheat, singleldouble pass, radiant furnace,
dry bottom, outdoor, pulverized coal-fired, balanced draft, outdoor type and
would use a direct firing system. Each steam generator would deliver 1,700
tonneslh of superheated steam at 179 kglcmt and 5400 C at the super heater
outlet, and 1,524 tonneslh of reheated steam at 540° C at the reheater outlet.
Each steam generator would be able to take full load (1002 maximum continuous
rating) when burning worst quality coal. Coal burners would be so designed
that a high combustion efficiency is achieved and the use of fuel oil is
minimized. Low sulphur heavy stock (LSHS) fuel oil would be used in
conjunction with pulverised coal firing during start-up and low loads for warm
up and flame stabilisation.
1.28      Electrostatic Precivitators (ESPs). Each steam generator would be
fitted with a high efficiency ESP having an efficiency that limits the outlet
emissions to 100 mg/ma while the boiler is operating at its maximum eontinuous
rating and burning worst coal having maximum ash content. Each ESP would have
four independently operated streams. The residual particulate matter in the
flue gas at the ESP outlet would be dispersed in the atmosphere through a
chimney. Space provision would be kept in the layout for addition of Flue Gas
Desulphurization (FGD) plant in the future, if required.
1.29      Chimney.  The emission of the fly ash would be through a twin flue,
reinforced concrete chimney of 275 m high common to the two units.

1.30      Turbines and Generators.  The two turbines of 500 MV each would be
of compound single reheat, regenerative, condensing, multi-cylinder design
with separate HP and IP casings. It would be directly coupled to a generator
suitable for indoor installation. The turbine would be suitable for variable
pressure operation. Each steam turbine would have the following steam
parameters: Turbine throttle steam pressures 170 kgicm 2; turbine throttle
steam and turbine reheat steam temperaturest 537° C; and maximum operating
turbine ezhaust pressures 89 mm Hg.  The two generators, each rated at 588

to meet plant requirement.
1.31      Ash Handling System and Ash Disposal Area.  Bottom ash would be

fly ash from electrostatic hoppers would be extracted and conveyed to
bufferisurge hoppers located adjacent to the ESP by means of pneumatic

storage silos for ash utilization purposes. In case of non-utilization of fly
ash, the fly ash from buffer surge hoppers would be slurrified and led to ash
slurry sump in ash slurry pump house for further disposal. Then the total ash
would be disposed of as a slurry through pipelines to the ash disposal area
which is located at approximately 5 km from the power station. After
sedimentation, ash free water would be allowed to discharge into the GBPS
reservoir. The discharged water would have a suspended solid concentration of
less than 100 mg/l.
